iv
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
While climate change continues to contribute to harmful algae blooms all over the world, the
Baltic Sea stands out as a highly susceptible region due to its brackish, shallow water and
the high nutrient inflow from rivers of the densely populated neighboring countries, leading to
eutrophication and overabundance of planktonic algae. Monitoring plankton populations can
provide early warning systems for resource managers to take proactive measures. Despite ex­
tensive research on monitoring plankton in the Baltic Sea using numerical models and historical
trends derived from merged remote sensing data, no research has investigated the usability of
deep learning models to predict the plankton populations based on remote sensing data. This
study aims to step into this space by investigating the convenience of deep learning models
to fill data gaps and predict the chlorophyll­a concentration, which directly relates to plankton
abundance in the Baltic Sea. Furthermore, the study seeks to assess the feature importance
of the multiple factors that affect plankton populations in this complex ecosystem.
To achieve these goals, multiple deep learning models were compared for both gap­filling and
chlorophyll­a concentration prediction in three predefined subareas: the Southern Kattegat,
Gotland Basin and Gulf of Riga. For the gap­filling, three types of autoencoder models (DINCAE,
DINpCAE, pCONV3D) were setup, with the pCONV3D model outperforming the DINCAE model
in accurately reconstructing the actual data. The output of the gap­filling model, in addition to
multiple oceanographic and atmospheric parameters, was utilized to predict the chlorophyll­a
concentration five days in advance. Two recursive deep learning models, ConvLSTM model
and ConvLSTM model with Self­Attention, were compared, with the simpler ConvLSTM model
exhibiting superior performance on the provided dataset. Optimization experiments conducted
on the Gulf of Riga area, encompassing different data sets, data transformations and data avail­
abilities, demonstrated that log transformation of the data prior to training helped to improve the
model performances. Moreover, training the models on more data, even if originating from
different data sources proved beneficial for overall performance.
Comparisons with baseline models revealed varied results for both the gap­filling and prediction
models. Based on the root mean square error, the gap­filling model performed 68% better than
ordinary kriging in the Gulf of Riga, but ­46% worse in the Gotland Basin. The prediction model
showed superior performance in the Gulf of Riga and Gotland Basin, with improvements of
11% and 3% in comparison to the historical average baseline, but a decreased performance of
­12% in the Kattegat region. Also, no definitive conclusion could be drawn regarding the feature
importance as different features exhibited varying degrees of significance across different areas.
This suggests the need for tailored model setups in different areas, considering the geographical
diversity and unique driving factors of the Baltic Sea. In contrast to the existing numerical model
forecasts, the presented deep learning approach displayed a higher similarity to in­situ samples
with an improvement of 72% based on the root mean square error, showcasing its potential for
predicting plankton populations in the Baltic Sea more accurately than current methods.
Moving forward, future research should focus on improving data quality through comprehensive
data exploration and analysing the impact of different preprocessing methods, such as data
smoothing, on overall model performance. Moreover, to further validate and strengthen the
findings, a larger­scale comparison to in­situ measurements should be performed.
In conclusion, this study outlines the potential of deep learning models to fill gaps in remote
sensing data and predict plankton populations. It further identifies areas of possible pitfalls,
such as unaccounted differences between datasets that call for further investigation, highlights
the importance of accounting for local differences when setting up deep learning models and
emphasises the need for ongoing efforts to improve data quality and preprocessing techniques.

Acknowledgements
I would like to express my special gratitude to my thesis supervisor, Associate Professor at
the Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science, Statistics and Data Analysis,
Anders Stockmarr, for his constant guidance and valuable support in the course of this project,
patiently answering my questions and queries and encouraging my work.
Another special thank you to PhD Henrik Andersson, my external supervisor from the company
DHI, for introducing me to the project, helping me to find a university supervisor, discussing my
queries and connecting me to different experts in the company. Overall, I want to thank the
company DHI and especially all employees of the Data and Analytics department for being so
interested in the development of my project and supporting me with their expertise in remote
sensing data and deep learning modelling.
Furthermore, I am grateful for my friends, near and far, who would always show keen interest
in my progress and cross­reading this thesis and who would comfort me when I was down. Be
it by sending motivational postcards and parcels, discussing priorities over puzzle sessions,
encouraging me to creep out of my cave­room and engage in different activities from time to
time or tackling the wavy waters of Bargsværd Sø together. Thank you for making the dullness
of everyday thesis writing a lot brighter.
Finally I want to thank my family and partner for all the emotional support during my studies,
keeping my spirits and motivation high and believing in me.

xviii
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:19==
1
Introduction
Phytoplankton provide the basis of the pelagic food web and play a crucial role in the ocean’s
ecosystems. Even though they are only microscopic plants that drift close to the ocean surface,
they make up 90% of the ocean productivity [1], [2] and produce a majority of the oxygen that we
breathe by performing photosynthesis [3]. However, when the phytoplankton abundance be­
comes too large, the natural balance is disturbed and the very same ecosystem that depends
on the plankton for its life, suffers of ecological degradation through hypoxia. This phenomenon
can be observed, when too many nutrients are available in the water, causing the phytoplankton
populations to grow excessively out of control. Given the strong positive correlation, plankton
populations can be interpreted as indicators of eutrophication levels of the ocean. With the on­
going of climate change and increased human pressure, nutrient loads and thus eutrophication
in some parts of the oceans have increased in recent years, leading to more harmful plankton
blooms [4], [5], [6].
The Baltic Sea is especially subsceptible to eutrophication, due to high human pressure in
addition to its shallowness and enclosure, that allows only very limited water inflow of oxygen­
rich water from the North Sea [7]. While the Baltic Sea was known to be a nutrient­low area
of the ocean up until the 1950s, with the ongoing of industrialisation in the farming industry,
fertilizer started to wash into the sea, leading to a drastic rise in nutrient loads and eutrophication
[8]. With increasing levels of phosphorus and nitrogen concentrations in the water, plankton
populations started to increase and blooms started to occur more frequently in vast parts of
the sea surface. When the short­lived phytoplankton dies and sinks to the ocean floor, it is
degraded by bacteria. These bacteria consume the water’s oxygen to create carbon dioxide
when decomposing the organic material, which leads to a depletion of oxygen in the water that
makes the area inhabitable for fish and many other sea animals, causing so called ’dead­zones’
[9]. While dead zones are deadly for the local ecosystem, they are also devastating for the local
fishing industry [5].
To assess the environmental status and climate change effects of the Baltic Sea, monitoring of
plankton populations is a crucial factor and part of the Danish Marine Strategy for a clean and
healthy environment [10] as well as various other policy frameworks of the surrounding countries
[11]. In 1974 the Baltic Marine Environment Protection Commission (HELCOM) was established
with the goal to protect the Baltic Sea. As part of this commission, the Cooperative Monitoring in
the Baltic Marine Environment was instituted in 1991 in order to identify and quantify the effects
of human pressure in the Baltic Sea and to observe changes in the environment [12].
So far, the monitoring happens mainly through in­situ measurements, supported by numerical
models. While the in­situ measurements provide precise information about the status­quo in the
exact measurement location, the station abundance and sample frequency is generally low. To
provide information and predictions of the whole area of the Baltic Sea, numerical models that
are calibrated based on in­situ data simulate the interaction of multiple factors that influence
plankton populations through mathematical models. These factors include atmospheric and
sea­surface temperature, waveheights, currents, sun­intensity, as well as the appearance of
organisms that feed on phytoplankton. However, these relationships are highly non­linear and
uncertain and depend on a multitude of diverse physical, chemical and biological factors, which
are not fully known [13]. To overcome these shortcomings, the presented project investigates
the usability of deep learning models to predict plankton populations in the Baltic Sea based on
remote­sensing data.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
1
==PAGE:20==
1.1
Motivation
The thesis is written in collaboration with the company DHI, experts in water modelling and
simulation. As part of their portfolio they are developing a numerical model of plankton popula­
tions in the Baltic Sea, to assist policy and decision making as well as environmental research
in the area. In order to improve the model performance, a possible approach is to assimilate
predictions of deep learning models based on satellite data into the numerical model. So far,
no predictive model utilizing remote sensing data over the Baltic Sea exists for this purpose, yet
multiple studies from other oceans have shown promising results to implement machine and
particularly deep learning models to model plankton dynamics [14], [15], [16]. Deep learning
models have the advantage that they are able to capture highly non­linear relationships and can
combine multiple data sources, such as remote sensing data. This spatial approach allows to
integrate information of an entire area into the model instead of just single locations as it is the
case when using in­situ measurements.
To detect plankton in satellite images, their green colour is utilized, that differentiates itself from
the blue color of the water. Their dominant pigment, chlorophyll­a (CHL) , that is responsible for
the green color can be interpreted as an indicator of plankton presence. The CHL concentration
can be extracted from satellite images by utilising the ocean­color spectrum that is calculated
from the multiple bands of a satellite. While the use of satellite imagery is very promising, a
large drawback, especially in the Baltic Sea, is the occurrence of cloud covers and gaps in the
data during the winter month that lead to patchy data that can not be used to perform reliable
predictions.
To address the before­mentioned problems, the presented project compares multiple deep
learning models to first fill the gaps in satellite­based data and then, based on the output, per­
forms predictions of the CHL concentration in three predefined subareas of the Baltic Sea,
namely the Southern Kattegat, Gotland Basin and Gulf of Riga. For the gap­filling, two types
of autoencoder models, Data INterpolating Convolutional AutoEncoder (DINCAE) and partially
CONvolutional 3D AutoEncoder (pCONV3D) are utilized and modified in an approach to find
the best model for gap­filling CHL data in the Baltic Sea. The output of this model in addition
to multiple oceanographic and atmospheric parameters is then used for the prediction of the
CHL concentration 5 days ahead, comparing the performance of two recursive deep learning
models, a common Convolutional Long Short Term Memory (ConvLSTM) and a Convolutional
Long Short Term Memory with Self­Attention (SaConvLSTM) model. Given the complexity of
the ecosystem and the multiple factors that affect plankton populations, the input features are
analysed in order to assess which features are most important for the CHL prediction with the
best performing deep learning model. The output of the best prediction model will be com­
pared to numerical model forecasts and a small selection of in­situ measurements, to access
the model performance.
By exploring the potential of deep learning models to fill gaps in satellite data and predict plank­
ton populations, this project aims to investigate the usability of deep learning models in the Baltic
Sea.
1.2
Research Questions and Objectives
In alignment with the project definition three research questions were defined prior to the start
of the project:
1. How effective is the use of deep learning models to fill gaps in the spatio­temporal data of
CHL concentration?
2. Can a deep learning model trained on time series of CHL data extracted from satellite
2
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:21==
imagery in addition to other relevant factors such as for example sea surface temperature
quantify plankton populations in the Baltic Sea more accurately than existing bio­chemical
models?
3. What are the main additional factors influencing the prediction of plankton through deep
learning models?
1.3
Structure
The thesis is structured into eight chapters. The first chapter introduces the problem at hand
and thereby motivates the project. The following chapter provides background information on
numerical biological modelling, remote sensing and deep learning, in order to provide the nec­
essary foundations that the project is build upon. In the third chapter an overview of related
work and the current state of the art in the investigated areas of gap­filling of spatio­temporal
data as well as the prediction of plankton populations is provided. The fourth chapter shows
the research area, introduces the data sources and provides background to the utilised data.
Furthermore, the preprocessing steps that were taken are outlined in this chapter. In the fifth
chapter the architecture of the utilized deep learning models for the gap­filling and prediction
are explained, which is followed by a chapter with a description of the experiments that were
performed on these models as well as the analysis of feature­importance. Additionally, the re­
sults of the comparison between the output of deep­learning models and numerical interpolation
and predictions with the in­situ data are shown. The seventh chapter discusses the presented
project and highlights further steps and drawbacks of the approached methods before a con­
clusion is drawn in the eighth chapter.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
3
==PAGE:22==
2
Theory and Background
The study combines multiple disciplines by using data with a remote sensing background, ap­
plying deep learning models on that data and comparing the results to numerical models, aiming
to predict plankton populations. The following chapter provides the theory and background of
these disciplines. While the focus of the project lies on the implementation of deep learning
methods for spatio­temporal data, in the following also a background on plankton dynamics in
the Baltic Sea and the basics of numerical ecological modelling as well as remote sensing are
provided. Furthermore, the fundamental concepts of the deep learning models that were imple­
mented in this project are introduced, in order to provide the foundation for understanding the
models.
2.1
Plankton Population Dynamics in the Baltic Sea
Plankton are small organisms that live in the oceans and are carried by currents and tides. Some
species are called plankton as infants until they grow and become stronger. They are considered
plankton as long as they are not able to swim against the tide and thus their location is solely
dependent on external factors, which is also represented in their name as ”plankton” is greek
for ”drifter”. Plankton can be divided into two groups, phytoplankton (plants) and zooplankton
(animals) [17]. This project will only address phytoplankton, which is a type of very small algae
that is known to be the bottom of the aquatic foodchain and thus play a cruical role for the marine
ecosystem.
Phytoplankton, being phototrophs, are very small plants living near the water surface, where
they are able to build up populations by performing photosynthesis. They utilize sunlight, carbon
dioxide, nitrogen, sulfur and phosphorus as well as other elements to create the energy they
need to survive, thereby indirectly converting carbon dioxide into oxygen. Given enough light
and nutrients are available, according to Bassit et al. [18] phytoplankton can have a turnover
rate of 1.4 to 2 per day in coastal marine environments and up to 10 times per day in brackish
ecosystems. The turnover rate states how fast the biomass is renewed or replenished, which
can imply a larger total growth rate of the population, given enough resources are available.
Furthermore, Bassit et al. [18] state that the ”biomass density of every single local popula­
tion is extremely variable and unpredictable in time and species richness is commonly much
higher than factors limiting phytoplankton fitness at every time and space”, which leads to the
conclusion that it is very hard to predict and forecast phytoplankton populations.
Nevertheless, when looking specifically at the Baltic Sea, annual patterns of an increase in
pythoplankton populations, so called plankton blooms, can be observed biannually. That is, a
spring bloom and a summer bloom, where the spring bloom is more dominant than the summer
bloom. Hjerne et al. [19] investigated the timing and factors causing the spring bloom by col­
lecting data from several fixed in­situ stations off the swedish coastline. Their results show that
the spring bloom is mainly caused by the increase in light after the winter months, especially in
more brackish and coastal areas, while the effect of warmer temperatures on the bloom is small.
However, runoff from rivers that lead into the Baltic Sea and wash nitrogen into the water can
also cause the spring blooms. The intensity of the bloom is mainly influenced by the amount
of nutrients that are already in the water before the bloom starts, yet can also be influenced
by a high light intensity in combination with cold water temperatures. They further show that
the spring bloom in the observed in­situ stations commonly starts in March and lasts for about
3 month, yet showing significant inter­annual variability. Beltran­Perez et al. [20] confirm the
observations of the bloom timings with experiments on a bio­physical model. Opposite to the
4
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:23==
results by Hjerne et al. [19], their experiments show, that in a simulated environment the sea
surface temperature has a significant effect on the duration and intensity of the blooms. They
further investigate that the summer bloom starts around the beginning of June and lasts for
approximately 2.5 month.
The species composition of phytoplankton in the Baltic Sea is very diverse, with approximately
more that 10000 individual species. The main taxonomic groups are diatoms, dinoflagellates
and cyanobacteria [20]. As described by Munkes et al. [21] an overload of cyanobacteria is es­
pecially dangerous for the ecosystem, as they are so called ’nitrogen­fixing’, which means that
they are able to convert dinitrogen that is present in the air to bio­available nitrogen, increasing
the risk of eutrophication and enhancing the growth of the other plankton species. Furthermore,
some cyanobacteria release toxins that, during a bloom, when the population is extremely large,
can cause the death of all other marine animals in the area [21]. Phytoplankton blooms usu­
ally collapse due to a combination of nutrient depletion in the water and increased feeding by
their predators, zooplankton, small fish and crustaceans like crabs, whose population increases
peridocially with the phytoplankton [3].
Overall, the phytoplankton dynamics in the Baltic Sea are a complex area of research, varying
from place to place and still causing a lot of open questions. Generally, two peaks in the phyto­
plankton population can be expected, one between March and May, and one between June and
August, during the spring and summer bloom. Furthermore, shallow, brackish areas are more
prone to larger and more variable phytoplankton populations than more open, deeper areas.
2.2
Numerical Ecological Modelling
An ecological ecosystem is a community of living organisms interacting with each other and their
physical environment. Ecological models are simplified representations of these systems that
allow a better understanding and making predictions about their dynamics and behavior. The
system is based on the exchange of mass and energy during the interaction of organisms with
their environment. It is setup of a multitude of parameters and variables that represent these
interactions mathematically. By changing these parameters it is easy to predict and estimate
the systems’ behaviour under different scenarios.
A numerical ecological model consists of the interaction of state variables, external forcing and
output variables. The state variables represent a time dependent variable, as the biomasses
of organisms or the concentrations of nutrients and are affected by external forcing functions,
that regulate the system yet are not directly calculated in the model. Such factors can be for
example water temperature, light and flow rates. In addition to the state variables, an ecological
model also has output variables, which are measured quantities that are used to compare the
model to reality. Taking phytoplankton populations as an example, the state variable would be
the phytoplankton biomass, while the output variable would be CHL. The interaction between
different state variables is called flow.
Figure 2.1 shows the flowchart of a very simplified ecosystem model, the NPZD (Nutrients (N),
Phytoplankton (P), Zooplankton (Z), Detritus (D)) model, that contains only four different state
variables, one forcing function (solar radiation (S)) and one output variable (CHL). The following
description follows Soetaert et al. [22]. Black arrows in the figure indicate the different flows
between the state variables, as described in the bottom of the figure.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
5
==PAGE:24==
Figure 2.1: NPZD Model, based on Soetaert et al. [22]
Based on the flowchart a conceptual model can be generated that defines at what rate the state
variables change, based on the inflow and outflow. This is described in Equation (2.1) to (2.4):
dP
dt = (a) −(b)
(2.1)
Z
df = (b) −(c) −(d) −(e)
(2.2)
D
dt = (c) + (e) −(f)
(2.3)

In a next step all flows are expressed as mathematical variables. Still following Soetaert et al.

(a) P uptake of N = MaxUp · min(

Eventhough the model is just a simple representation of the system, it already includes 10 pa­
rameters that must be defined to setup the model. The Monod formulation for light dependency
ksPAR limits the light availability, the Monod formulation for nutrient update ksDIN limits the
nutrient uptake and the Monod formulation for grazing limitation ksP limits the grazing that is
dependent on the phytoplankton population. MaxUp is the physiological limitation of nutrient
intake for each phytoplankton individual and MaxGrazing is the physiological limitation of how
6
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:25==
much phytoplankton a zooplankton can graze. pFaeces is the parameter of constant fraction of
total grazing and excretionRate the excretion rate parameter. The parameters mortalityRate
and mineralizationRate are influenced by external organisms and bacteria that are not part of
this simple model and the rate is thus hard­coded. Finally, the phytoplankton is converted to
the output variable CHL, by defining a constant proportionality factor ChlNratio between the
phytoplankton and CHL concentrations.
To define the parameters correctly, model outputs can be compared to in­situ data and ad­
justed accordingly. Furthermore, in­situ and satellite data can be assimilated into the model, to
increase the model performance.
The presented NPZD model gives a sense of the complexity that is necessary to model whole
ecosystems in more depth using numerical ecological modelling and also shows the vulnerability
of this method, due to assumptions in parameters and simplifications in interactions that must
be made.
2.3
Deep Learning
Opposite to the numerical models, machine learning models do not attempt to simulate a whole
ecosystem by modelling the interactions between variables, but extract features directly from the
data that they are provided with. Taking the previous example with CHL as the output variable,
the machine learning model can take features that describe the ecosystem conditions, such as
water temperature, solar radiation and previous CHL concentration as input and then output the
estimation of the current CHL concentration.
Deep learning is a subfield of machine learning that, inspired by the structure of the human
brain, automatically learns hierarchical representations of raw data, given enough time and
computing power are provided. Deep learning models are able to discover intricate structures
and dependencies even in large datasets by extracting meaningful features from raw input data
through multiple interconnected layers. The most common type of deep learning is supervised
learning, where the model learns by mapping the reconstructed output to an expected output.
Artificial Neural Networks (ANN) provide the foundation for deep learning models. They consist
of interconnected nodes, known as neurons, that are structured into layers. Each neuron is
provided with inputs, performs a weighted sum, applies an activation function and then passes
the output on to other neurons. The activation function plays a crucial role in the process, as it
introduces non­linearity to the model and enables it to map the input space into higher dimen­
sional feature spaces. During the so called training, the weights and biases of the model are
iteratively adjusted in order to minimize a predefined loss function. This optimization process is
done through backpropagation, a process in which the derivative of the loss function in respect
to the model parameters is calculated and the model parameters are updated to follow a neg­
ative gradient direction that thus reduces the loss, applying the chain rule from output to input
by multiplying the derivatives of each layer. Mathematically this can be expressed by:
θt+1 = θt −α · δL(X, θt)
δθ
(2.12)
where θ includes all weights wk
ij for each node i in layer lk and node j in layer lk−1 and biases
bk
i for node i in layer lk. L(X, θt) is the loss function in respect to the expected output X and
the model parameters θt at iteration t. δL(X,θt)
δθ
is the derivative of the loss function that has to
be calculated with respect to all weights and biases [23] .
In a deep learning model, multiple layers of neurons are stacked together, forcing the information
to flow through multiple hidden layers, that are neither input nor output layer. This increased
complexity can help the model to learn more complex representations of the data. However,
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
7
==PAGE:26==
when the network becomes too complex, the so called ’vanishing gradient problem’ can block
the learning process of the model when many small derivatives are multiplied together and this
way the gradient decreases exponentially from output to input layer, causing the initial layers
to not update their weights effectively. This can lead to inefficiency of the whole network. To
overcome this problem many solutions have been proposed, as for example using alternative
activation functions that do not cause very small gradients, applying batch normalization and
adding skip connections to the model. [24]
For the example of the CHL prediction, the model thus extracts only the relevant information
from the input features and generates a prediction based on these features. This predicted
current CHL concentration is then compared to the known, true CHL concentration, based on a
predefined loss function. The closer the predicted concentration is to the true concentration, the
smaller the loss of the model. The weights of the network are then updated, aiming to reduce the
loss function and thus generate an output that is as close to the true concentration as possible.
Therefore, the model output is fit to and relies heavily on the data that was used to train the
model. However, mostly the model is supposed to perform well also on unknown data, that the
model was not priory trained on. To ensure that the model does not overfit to the training data,
regularization techniques can be applied that add noise to the model and thus hinder the model
to extract features that are only relevant for the specific training dataset.
2.3.1
Optimizers
Different optimizers can be applied in the model to update the weights. The Adaptive Moment
Estimation (ADAM) optimizer by Kingma et al. [25] is an extended version of the gradient de­
scent algorithm that optimizes the weights based on first and second order moments. The algo­
rithm uses these moments to keep track of the exponentially decaying average of past gradients
and past square gradients, using two parameters β1 and β2 to control the decay rates of the
moving averages. In each iteration the gradients in respect to the loss function are computed
using backpropagation. Then, the moments are updated based on the calculated gradients,
taking the weighted average of the previous moments and the current gradient and squared
current gradient, based on the parameters β1 and β2. After applying a bias correction, the
weights and biases in the network are updated by subtracting the update parameters that are
calculated by dividing the first moment by the squared second moment for each parameter and
multiplying the result with the learning rate. This way, the learning rate is adjusted for every in­
dividual parameter. The process is repeated for every iteration in the model training, gradually
optimizing the model parameters.
2.3.2
Activation Functions
As mentioned before, activation functions are a crucial part of every neural network, as they
introduce non­linearity to the model. The choice of activation function is model­specific and
can be considered a hyper parameter of the model. For the hidden layers the most common
activation functions are the sigmoid function (σ), Rectified Linear Unit (ReLU) and hyperbolic
tangent (tanh). They are the most famous, because they introduce nonlinearity to the model
while still being easily differentiable. The ReLU activation function has become the standard
in recent years, as it is less prone to the vanishing gradient problem [26]. For the output, a
suitable activation function must be found that is able to capture the desired output range for a
regression task. For example for output values larger than 1, the tanh and σ activation function
are unsuitable and for negative values the ReLU function is unsuitable. Therefore, the common
choice for regression tasks is to use a simple linear activation function in the output layer [26].
8
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:27==
Sigmoid (σ)
The σ activation function converts any real input value to a value between 0 and 1. Where larger
input values are closer to 1 and smaller input values closer to 0.
σ =
1
1 + e−1
(2.13)
Rectified Linear Unit (ReLU)
The ReLU activation function returns the input value for values larger than 0 and 0 for negative
values. This method includes the desirable characteristics of a linear function for positive values,
yet it is not linear, which allows the network to learn more complex non­linear relations.
ReLU = max(0.0, x)
(2.14)
An alternative to the ReLU activation function is the Leaky Rectified Linear Unit (LeakyReLU)
activation function, that instead of setting negative values hard to zero, multiplies negative val­
ues with a small custom value α and this way breaks the linearity of the output.
LeakyReLU = max(α · x, x)
(2.15)
Hyperbolic Tangent (tanh)
The tanh activation function is similar to the σ activation function in the sense, that the function
converts any real input value to a range, in the case of tanh between ­1 and 1. For the very
small values, the function saturates to ­1 and for very large values to 1.
tanh = ex −e−x
ex + e−x
(2.16)
Linear
The linear activation function simply returns the sum of the input values, without performing any
more activations. While this is not desirable in the hidden layers, as then no non­linear features
can be identified, it is required for the output layer of a regression task when the value range is
larger than ­1 to 1.
linear = x
(2.17)
2.3.3
Regularization Techniques
Regularization techniques are used to avoid overfitting of the model to the training data. Other
than the activation function, regularization techniques do not have to be included but are optional
additions to deep learning models that can improve the model performance. Multiple types of
regularization exist. In the following, the ones that were applied in the project are introduced,
following [27] and [28].
L1 and L2 Regularization
The L1 and L2 regularization are very similar regularization techniques. Both techniques add a
penalty term to the loss function. In the L1 regularization, also called Lasso regularization, the
penalty term is the sum of absolute weight values multiplied by a predefined parameter λ.
L1 = loss + λ
W
X
i=1
|βi|
(2.18)
This technique pushes less important weights in the model towards zero which can lead to
feature selection if βi is chosen to be large, as the model then ignores less relevant features.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
9
==PAGE:28==
In the L2 regularization, also called ridge regularization, the penalty term is the sum of squared
weight values multiplied by a predefined parameter λ.
L2 = loss + λ
W
X
i=1
(βi)2
(2.19)
By adding the squared value, the technique encourages the model to have smaller weight values
overall, which has a smoothing effect on the weight values that can help to prevent over­fitting.
Batch Normalization
Batch normalization is a technique introduced by Ioffe et al. [29] in which normalization is applied
to each learning batch by subtracting the batch mean and dividing the batch standard deviation
before passing the output on to the next layer. Due to the constant weight updating in the
network, the distribution of the input to each layer changes during training, which makes it
hard for the model to find an optimal weight setup. By normalizing the input to each layer, the
distribution is stabilized which results in faster learning rates. Furthermore, as the normalization
is happening for each batch, some random variation is introduced to the model that can be
interpreted as noise that is introduced to the hidden layers during training. This helps the model
to generalize better and avoid overfitting to the training data.
2.3.4
Convolutional Neural Network (CNN)
The project deals solely with spatial data. Therefore, one dimensional neural networks are not
well suited for the problem, as these networks can not easily include spatial information to the
model. A solution to this problem is to use CNNs. CNNs, first introduced by LeCun et al. [30]
are a type of deep learning model that is capable of extracting spatial information of two or three
dimensional data. The core functionality of the CNN lies in the convolutions, that carry the main
computational load of the network.
A convolution works by calculating the dot product between the multi dimensional data and a
matrix of learnable parameters, the kernel, that defines the receptive region. During a forward
pass of the model, the kernel is passed along the image with a given stride and produces a
so­called feature map. This process is repeated multiple times with different kernels to extract
Figure 2.2: Concept of convolutions, showing a two dimensional convolutional layer with a kernel of size (3x3), a
stride of size 2 and zero padding applied around the image. The dot product is calculated for each kernel window
and the kernel than moved over the image, according to the predefined stride, resulting in the feature map.
10
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:29==
different features of the image. The resulting feature maps are then all stacked together and
form the output of the convolutional layer, that is passed through the non­linear activation func­
tion. In order to maintain the dimensions of the image, zero padding can be applied, whereby a
frame of zeros is applied around the multi­dimensional matrix. While the kernels are automati­
cally learned during the training process, the stride, kernel size and padding are hyperparame­
ters that have to be predefined. [31]
Figure 2.2 shows an example of a two dimensional convolutional layer with a kernel of size
(3x3), a stride of size 2 and zero padding applied around the image. Using a stride of size 2
reduces the image size and is useful as a downsampling procedure to reduce the dimensionalty
of the image. An alternative to a larger stride size is a process called pooling, where a patch of
images is reduced to the maximal (max­pooling), minimal (min­pooling) or average (average­
pooling) number within the patch, moving the patch along the image with a predefined stride
size. Figure 2.3 illustrates the max­pooling downsampling technique with a stride of 2. Most
commonly the operation is performed after the convolution on the feature map, reducing the
width and height yet maintaining the depth of the map. [31]
Figure 2.3: Concept of max pooling
2.3.5
Auto­Encoders
The Auto­Encoder model provides the basis for the models used in the gap­filling part of the
project. An Auto­Encoder is a deep learning network that attempts to reconstruct the input
to the output, which makes it especially interesting for any reconstruction tasks. It was first
introduced by Hochreiter et al. [32] for dimensionality reduction of data. An Auto­Encoder
typically consists of an encoder part that reduces the dimensionality and extracts the relevant
features, a bottleneck that consists of the compressed features and limits the flow of information,
thus only passing on the essential features and a decoder part that attempts to reconstruct the
input based on the compressed feature representation. The concept of a simple auto­encoder
is visualized in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Concept of Auto­Encoders
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
11
==PAGE:30==
When several layers are used to encode and decode the features, the auto­encoder is consid­
ered a deep auto­encoder. An example of a deep auto­encoder is the U­net, that was introduced
by Ronneberger et al. [33].
A U­net consists of the same three parts as an auto­encoder, the encoder, bottleneck and de­
coder. To reduce the dimensionality in the encoder, multiple convolutions with data reduction
either through pooling or kernel strides are implemented down to the bottleneck. The bottleneck
is the narrowest part of the network that limits the flow of information and forces the network to
only pass the essential information. In the decoder part the data is then upscaled again using
interpolation until it has the initial shape. However, due to the increased depth of the network,
the vanishing gradient problem can occur. Furthermore, in some cases it can happen that too
much information gets missing when passing the information through the bottleneck. That is
why in the U­Net structure, skip connections are applied that combine the filtered information
from the decoder layer and the initial information from the encoder layer. The combining can
be done either by concatenating the two layers or by adding them together. Figure 2.5 shows
the conceptual setup of a U­Net structure, with the different operations, convolution, dimension­
ality reduction, dimensionality increase and skip connection. The dimensionality increase can
thereby already include a convolution to reduce the feature dimension.
Figure 2.5: Concept of U­Net structure
2.3.6
Long Short Term Memory (LSTM) Models
While the auto­encoders using convolutional layers are well suited to capture spatial and partly
short­term temporal information, they are not able to memorize long­term dynamics in the data.
To include these information, recurrent neural networks have been introduced. LSTM models
are a type of recurrent neural network that were introduced by Hochreiter et al. [34] to over­
come the vanishing gradient problem, and are mainly applied for the prediction of time series.
Figure 2.6 shows the setup of a LSTM model and cell. The model can be interpreted as a re­
cursive alignment of cells for each time step, as visualized on top of the figure for three time
steps. The output of one cell is the input of the next. Thereby, feedback connections allow the
model to memorize data from multiple timesteps and include this information in the prediction,
if considered relevant. Three factors influence the prediction when using an LSTM model. The
cell state, that captures the long­term dependencies (long­term), the hidden state, that captures
information about the previous point in time (short term) and the input data of the current time
step. How much these factors influence the predictions is determined with the help of three
different gates in the model, the forget, input and output gate. These gates are neural networks
themselves and can be interpreted as information filters.
The weight matrices corresponding to the input state xt are denoted as Wi, Wf, Wo, and Wc′,
12
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:31==
respectively, while the weight matrices for the hidden state ht−1 are denoted as Ui, Uf, Uo, and
Uc′. This separation of weights allows the model to learn different relationships between the
input and hidden states.
Figure 2.6: LSTM cell architecture

defines which part of the cell state (long­term memory) should be kept, based on the data of
the previous hidden state ht−1 (short term memory) and the current data xt at timestep t. To do
so, the previous hidden state and current data are passed into a neural network that uses a σ
activation function (2.13).

between 0 and 1, with 1 being very important and 0 not­important information. By multiplying
the previous cell state with the forget vector, as done in Equation (2.23), the long­term memory
information is thus filtered based on the current and previous information.

The network creates another filter vector with values between 0 and 1, that is then pointwise
