Instead of the tanh also other activation functions can be used within the LSTM model, such as
ReLU or LeakyReLU.
2.3.7
ConvLSTM Model
The ConvLSTM is the fundamental model used for the prediction part in this project. The model
was introduced by Shi et al. [36] to include spatial information to the LSTM model by combining
the CNN and LSTM models. Just like the LSTM model, the ConvLSTM is a recursive model,
yet instead of internal matrix multiplications, convolutional operations are used that allow the
model to take inputs of more than just one dimension and makes the model especially useful
for the prediction of spatio­temporal data.
Instead of cell, hidden state and gate 1D vectors, 3D tensors are used whose last two dimen­
sions are the spatial dimensions of the data. As Shi et al. [36] explain, this can be imagined as
14
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:33==
vectors standing on a grid. The future state of one grid cell is then determined by the input and
past states of its surrounding grid cells, which is achieved through convolutions. Following Shi
et al. [36] the mathematical formulas for the gates can thus be updated to be:
ft = σ (bf + Wx,f ∗xt + Wh,f ∗ht−1 + Wc,f · ct−1)
(2.26)
it = σ (bi + Wx,i ∗xt + Wh,i ∗ht−1 + Wc,i · ct−1)
(2.27)
c′
t = tanh
 bc′Wx,c′ ∗xt + Wh,c′ ∗ht−1

(2.28)
ct = ft · ct−1 + it · c′
t
(2.29)
ot = σ (bo + Wx,o ∗xt + Wh,o ∗ht−1 + Wc,o · ct)
(2.30)
ht = ot · tanh(ct)
(2.31)
The equations to calculate the outputs of the different gates in the ConvLSTM model are almost
identical to those of the LSTM model, with the equations to calculate the output of the forget
gate: (2.20) and (2.26), the equations to calculate the output of the input gate: (2.21) and (2.27),
the equations to calculate the cell state updating gate: (2.22) and (2.28), the equations to define
the new cell state: (2.23) and (2.29), the equations to calculate the output gate: (2.24) and (2.30)
and finally equations to calculate the new hidden state: (2.25) and (2.31).
Instead of dot products, the ConvLSTM model uses convolutions. In Equation (2.26), (2.27),
(2.28) and (2.30), the convolutions are denoted with * and are applied on both the input state
xt and the hidden state ht−1. Since both the input and hidden states go through convolutions,
a single set of weight matrices is used for each gate and the memory update cell. The weight
matrices are denoted as Wx,i, Wx,f, Wx,o, Wx,c′ for the input state convolutions, and Wh,i, Wh,f,
Wh,o, Wh,c′ for the hidden state convolutions. Furthermore, the previous memory cell ct−1 is
included in the input and forget gate, to capture previous spatial dependencies.
Depending on the speed of change of the observed data, the kernel sizes within the convolutions
can be adapted, with smaller kernels capturing faster movements and larger kernels capturing
slower movements.
2.4
Remote Sensing
The project relies heavily on data that was collected using remote sensing sensors. While there
are individual local in­situ stations that collect information about CHL concentrations in the Baltic
Sea, earth observation data can gather information about the entire area of the Baltic Sea at
once. Remote sensing can be defined as ”the science of gathering spatial information about the
Earth’s surface (as well as the oceans and atmosphere) from a distance, using either handheld,
aircraft, or satellite sensors” [37]. The resulting data can be differentiated depending on where
the sensors are mounted. To generate land­borne data, the sensor is mounted to an object on
land such as for example a tower, for air­borne data the sensor is mounted for example to an
airplane or drone and for space­borne data the sensor is mounted to a satellite or space ship
that orbits the earth.
Geostationary satellites are fixed at a certain longitude in relation to earth and rotate at the same
velocity as the earth, so that they seem motionless and can monitor one constant area while
polar­orbiting satellites orbit around the earth and continuously pass the same places on earth
after a certain period of time.
The sensors that are used in remote sensing can be categorized into active and passive sen­
sors. Active sensors act like a flashlight that sends out a beam of light to illuminate the area or
object they are observing. The sensor then detects the light that bounces back from the target,
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
15
==PAGE:34==
which helps to create an image or measurement of the object or area being observed. The ICE­
SAT missions by NASA [38] for example utilises a Geoscience Laser Altimeter System (GLAS)
instrument to collect altimetry data by measuring the elevation of the earth that lies on the track
of the satellite in a point­wise manner. Passive sensors detect natural radiation that originates
from the object being observed or that is reflected by the object from a source other than the
sensor itself. Passive sensors include radiometers, imaging radiometers and spectrometers,
that can be be used to generate 2D images of the observed scene in multiple bands, based
on the electromagnetic radiation in the visible, infrared, or microwave spectral region [39]. This
project utilizes data from multiple missions that include passive sensors, as for example the
Sentinel 3 mission, that is equipped with both active and passive sensors to provide land and
ocean observations. The Ocean and Land Colour Instrument (OLCI) is a imaging spectrometer
consisting of 5 cameras, that measure the solar radiation reflected by the earth in 21 spectral
bands [40].
To utilize remote sensing image data, it is important to be aware of the image resolutions. That
is the spatial resolution (the area on land that a single pixel in the image captures), the over­
all spatial extend (the total area that the image encaptures), the temporal resolution (the time
between image aquisitions), the radiometric resolution (the intensity of the image) and finally
the spectral resolution (the number, width and location of the wavelength band that can be de­
tected by the sensor). Usually, the more bands an image includes, the smaller the range of
wavelength, the bandwidth, of each band. [37]
Remote sensing data is categorized in four different levels, based on the amount of post­
processing applied to the data. These levels range from the raw level 0 data to the highly
post­processed level 4 data [39], [41]:
• Level 0: electromagnetic satellite sensor signals
• Level 1: mechnical characteristics of satellite data, to evaluate satellite position and data
imperfections (bands)
• Level 2: scientific data extracted from level 1 data, for example through atmospheric cor­
rection algorithms
• Level 3: combined level 2 data, merging data of multiple satellite bypasses of an area to
daily or monthly averages
• Level 4: highly post­processed level 3 data, applying data augmentation, interpolation,
resampling or rescaling to yield geophysical variables
In this study Level 2 to Level 4 data is utilized. Thus the geophysical characteristics are already
extracted from the raw data through atmospheric correction algorithms. This is further explained
in Chapter 4.
16
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:35==
3
Related Work
The monitoring and prediction of environmental phenomena have gained significant attention
in recent years, with extensive research conducted on climate change effects and methods
to understand and predict environmental phenomena. While there are numerous studies for
the Baltic Sea area that assess the development of plankton distribution over time, utilizing
remote sensing data from different sources ([42], [43], [44], [45]), no study that explicitly predicts
plankton populations based on remote sensing data could be found.
In this chapter the state of the art in two research fields that are highly relevant to the presented
project are reviewed. Those are methodologies to (1) fill gaps in satellite­ and especially spatio­
temporal satellite data with a focus on deep learning approaches to fill gaps in oceanographic
data and (2) existing models to predict plankton populations, in the Baltic Sea and around the
world.
3.1
Gap­Filling of Remote Sensing Data
One of the primary challenges when working with satellite­derived data is the sparsity caused
by atmospheric conditions such as for example cloud covers. To address this issue, a multitude
of methodologies have been introduced to fill gaps in satellite data. These methodologies aim
to reconstruct missing information and overcome data sparsity.
Different approaches of gap­filling methodologies can be categorized. Desai et al. [46] and
Zeng et al. [47] differentiate between single source, multi­source and hybrid methods while
Weiss et al. [48] and Gerber et al. [49] differentiate between spatial, temporal and spatio­
temporal models. In the following it will be assumed that the former terminology includes, but
is not reduced to the latter, so that spatial modelling is part of the single source class, temporal
modelling is part of the multi­source class and spatio­temporal modelling is part of the hybrid
class.
3.1.1
Single Source Models
Single source modelling imputes missing data based on available pixels within the same image.
Given that satellite images are taken at a certain point in time and capture information in both
the latitude and longitude dimension, filling gaps in satellite images using only one single picture
can be considered a spatial method, as the gap is only filled based on information in other spatial
dimensions.
Spatial models are mainly based on different interpolation techniques between the pixels that
surround the gap (Zhang et al. [50], Zhang et al. [51]). Ordinary Kriging (OK) (Rossi et al.
[52]) is a long established interpolation technique in geostatistics with multiple applications in
removing gaps in satellite imagery (Addink et al. [53], Kostopoulou [54]). It is a statistical
interpolation technique that is based on the first law of geostatistics, the principle that a missing
pixel is more similar to spatially close pixels in relation to those that are spatially further away
(Tobler [55]). A similar geostatistical model is the Geostatistical Neighborhood Similar Pixel
Interpolator (GNSPI), where not only the spatial distance but also the similarity (environmental
characteristics) of neighbouring pixels is taken into account when calculating the weights for the
interpolation (Zhu et al. [56], Chen et al. [57]).
Alternative single source approaches include the use of image processing techniques, such
as for example homomorphic filtering (Shen et al. [58]), image impainting (Maheshwari and
Krishnapriya [59]) and machine learning methods (Tong et al. [60]). Schonenberg and Kluiver
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
17
==PAGE:36==
[61] have recently generated a cloud detection and impainting method called CloudGAN that is
based on the image impainting method SN­PatchGAN (Yu et al. [62], [63]). The methodology
is targeted for RGB satellite images and implements first an autoencoder network to detect
clouds and then the SN­PatchGAN model to fill the cloud gaps. The SN­PatchGAN model
applies generative image impainting, supported by contextual attention and gated convolutions
to fill gaps in images. In the two folded methodology, the missing pixels are first coarsely filled
with an autoencoder­decoder model and then refined using two encoder layers in parallel, one
assigning attention to different parts of the image, before being concatenated and passed to one
decoder layer for the final results. Previously, Enomoto et al. [64] have introduced Multispectral
conditional Generative Adversarial Networks (McGANS), an extension of the two dimensional
conditional cGAN Generative Adversarial Networks (Mirza et al. [65]), as a method to remove
clouds from multispectral satellite images. Lee et al. [66] introduced a multi­scale convolutional
neural network, trained on masked images using real cloud­masks that were extracted from
cloudy days.
While single source models have the advantage of producing good results with less computing
power, they are limited in their ability to incorporate temporal information and perform poorly
when dealing with large spatial gaps in the data (Hu et al. [67], Þórðarson et al. [68]).
3.1.2
Multi Source Models
Multi­source methods derive the information from other available images to reconstruct missing
pixels. An example of such methods are temporal methods, where the missing pixel infor­
mation is filled based on available information for the same pixel at different time steps. For
instance, Hippert­Ferrer et al. [69] introduced Expectation Maximization­Empirical Orthogo­
nal Functions (EM­EOF), which iteratively reconstruct a time series by solving the eigenvalue
problem and thereby decomposing the temporal covariance into multiple Empirical Orthogonal
Function (EOF) modes, choosing the optimal number for the reconstruction. The Consistent
Adjustment of the Climatology to Actual Observations (CACAO) model by Verger et al. [70]
makes use of seasonal patterns to fill gaps in satellite time series data. The gaps are filled
based on the phenology model of every pixel. Seasonal climatological patterns are scaled and
shifted to the actual observations in order to fill the missing values.
Other multi­source methods include using multiple bands or images from different sensors to
fill the missing data. Spatial­spectral random forests (Wang et al. [71]) use the information of
multiple bands from known satellite images to simultaneously fill gaps based on random forests.
Similarly, Cokriging methods (Zhang et al. [72], Pringle et al. [73]) that are an extension of the
OK methods, use a second image of the same place to derive the missing information and fill
the gaps.
3.1.3
Hybrid Models
In hybrid models, the combination of single­ and multi­source methods, including spatio­temporal
models, allows for the generation of missing pixel values by integrating both temporal and spatial
information. These methodologies are particularly useful for reconstructing data from dynamic
earth observations, as for example weather and ocean parameters. As a result, numerous
spatio­temporal approaches have been developed to address data gaps in satellite data.
One example is the work of Hippert­Ferrer et al. [74] who extended the EM­EOF model to in­
clude an augmented third, spatial dimension, incorporating spatial information into the model.
Another approach is the gapfill model by Gerber et al. [75], a gap­filling algorithm, that ranks
images of a spatio­temporal subset around the gap based on a scoring algorithm and estimates
the missing values through quantile regression. Data INterpolating Empirical Orthogonal Func­
tion (DINEOF), introduced by Ćatipović et al. [41], is a widely used methodology to fill gaps in
oceanographic data. It utilizes EOF decomposition to iteratively calculate and replace missing
18
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:37==
values until a convergence criterion is met. The number of EOFs used is determined through
cross­validation.
Recent advancements in deep learning have also led to the development of various models
for filling cloud gaps in satellite data.
For example, Wang et al.
[76] proposed a content­
sequence­texture generation (CSTG) model, that combines a content generation model to ini­
tially fill gaps based on historical information from other images and a sequence texture model
to learn the dynamic changes. Sarukkai et al. [77] presented the spatio­temporal generative
network (STGAN), which combines the image­to­image impainting methods pix2pix (Isola et al.
[78]) and CycleGAN (Zhu et al. [79]) to fill gaps in infrared and RGB images. They compare
the use of a branched ResNet (He [80]) and U­Net architecture as the generator, whereby the
ResNet architecture outperforms the implemented U­Net architecture. In the branched ResNet,
images of each time step are passed to separate encoder­decoder layers to learn feature maps
that are then concatenated and passed to following encoder­decoder layers. The branched
U­Net architecture similarly encodes each image of all time steps separately before decoding
them together.
However, these methods assume that the previous time step is highly similar to the current time
step, making them less appropriate for filling gaps in dynamic systems. To address this limita­
tion, Daniels et al. [81] recently introduced a LSTM­CNN­Autoencoder Model to fill cloud gaps
of dynamic one­band satellite data of aerosol optical depths using mask­based attention maps
that focus the model attention on non­corrupted data. In their approach the gaps are first pre­
liminary filled using ConvLSTM models and then passed through a convolutional autoencoder
to improve the reconstruction. While this method successfully fills all gaps in the provided data
based on five previous time steps, it requires a substantial amount of cloud­free data for training
and validation and can only fill gaps for one day at a time.
For large­scale application of gap­filling to enable the use of the data for following analysis meth­
ods, Appel [82] implemented a 3D partial CNN (STpCNN), based on the 2D image impainting
methodology of Gruber et al. [83]. The method utilizes a U­Net architecture and passes a cloud
mask along through the network, which is impainted through convolutions, allowing the model
to focus only on relevant data in the time­block and filling the gaps in every time­step. This
methodology enables fast gap­filling and is suitable for large time­series datasets with sparse
data.
In the domain of CHL concentration gap­filling using machine learning, various methods have
been investigated. For instance, Park et al. [84] utilized a random forest trained on multiple
parameters including spatial and temporal information to reconstruct missing data of CHL con­
centration in the Ross Sea. Mohebzadeh [85] compared several machine learning approaches,
including Support Vector Regression, K­nearest neighbors, Random Forest Regression, and an
artificial neural network, and reported that all machine learning implementations outperformed
the DINEOF method mentioned earlier.
In 2020 the first implementation of the DINCAE Barth et al. [86]) was introduced, a neural net­
work model that is trained on the maximum likelihood of the observed variable. Initially applied
to sea surface temperature, DINCAE demonstrates superior performance in filling gaps in CHL
concentration data compared to DINEOF, as shown by Han et al. [87]. Similar to the STpCNN
method, DINCAE applies an autoencoder network and does not require cloud­free data for train­
ing. Instead, it leverages available data and incorporates training and validation masks to guide
the training process and model validation. A refined version of this model, DINCAEv2, was
subsequently introduced by Barth et al. [88], demonstrating optimized performance by utilizing
a more sophisticated autoencoder structure.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
19
==PAGE:38==
Overall, the field of gap­filling spatio­temporal satellite data is diverse, with various methodolo­
gies and techniques being explored. Each method has its strengths and limitations, and the
choice of approach depends on the specific requirements and characteristics of the dataset
and application at hand. Based on this review of state­of­the­art gap­filling methodologies, the
STpCNN and DINCAE model are chosen to be applied and extended to fit the requirements
of the project and then compared, to find a workable model to fill the data­gaps in the CHL
concentration in the Baltic Sea.
3.2
Prediction of Plankton Populations
To predict plankton populations two approaches exist. One is the setup of numerical models
to calculate the dynamic behavior of the plankton communities and the other uses data driven
models, based on historical in­situ measurements or remote sensing data.
3.2.1
Numerical Models
As explained previously in Section 2.2, numerical models are employed to simulate the growth
and distribution of plankton through mathematical relationships that represent the underlying
physical and biological processes. These models are not constrained by time nor space, as
they consists of mathematical formulations and parameterization based on assumptions. The
calibration and optimization of numerical models rely on comparing the model output to ob­
served data.
In the context of predicting plankton populations, numerical models can be divided into three
sub components. The physical component includes factors such as temperature, salinity and
nutrient transport and describes the circulation and mixing of water. The biochemical compo­
nent simulates the cycling of nutrients that are essential for plankton growth, such as nitrogen
and phosphorus and the dynamical component accounts for how plankton interact with their
environment, encompassing factors such as growth, mortality, and species grazing.
Several numerical models have been developed for the Baltic Sea, including BALTSEM [89],
ECOSMO [90], RCO­SCOBI [91] and SPBEM [92]. These models vary in terms of boundary
conditions, sub­basin flow, species interaction and the factors they incorporate [93]. The most
widely utilized bio­chemical model for the Baltic Sea is the ERGOM model by Neumann et al.
[94], which is coupled to the physical NEMO model by Madec et al. [95].
While numerical models are powerful to predict and understand ecological processes, they are
simplifications of complex ecosystems that are based on assumptions and may therefore not
fully capture all relevant processes and include initial errors. To overcome this limitation, data
assimilation is used to reanalyze the model predictions. In this approach, in­situ and satellite
observations are integrated into the model outputs, in order to partially enforce ground­truth
information and increase the model accuracy. NASA’s Ocean Biogeochemical Model (NOBM)
[96] is a recent example that assimilates ocean color CHL concentration data from multiple
satellites using the Relaxation Analysis Method CRAM [97]. This sequential adjustment of the
model output based on satellite data has demonstrated promising results in predicting global
ocean circulation and dynamics, yet entirely masks out brackish and coastal areas such as the
Baltic Sea.
3.2.2
Data­Driven Models
Data­driven models, such as machine learning models on the other hand are based on historical
data, that is utilized to train the model. This enables them to identify patterns and relationships
also in complex datasets without being dependent on prior assumptions. However, the perfor­
mance of data­driven models is highly limited by the extension and quality of the training data,
which can be affected by human bias.
20
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:39==
To predict plankton populations, the training data can either be the output of monitoring in­situ
stations or originate from satellite observations. The benefits of using data from monitoring
stations is on the one hand the certainty of truth of the collected data and on the other hand the
ability to gather not only surface data, but also data from beneath the water column. Per contra,
monitoring stations are often roughly and non­uniformly scattered around the area of interest
and thus only provide very punctual data. Furthermore, sampling happens mainly irregularly
and thus the data is both, temporal and spatially scattered, which makes it hard to capture all
spatio­temporal relationships. Therefore, predictions based on monitoring data are often one­
dimensional time­series analyses, without taking any spatial information into account. Rajaee et
al. [98] compare five different time series analysis methods to predict CHL concentration in the
South San Francisco Bay one month ahead. They compare the methods of wavelet analysis,
genetic algorithms, simple feed­forward neural network, support vector regression and multi
linear regression. Their study shows that in their area of research, wavelet analysis shows the
highest accuracy for single point CHL predictions. Shetty et al. [99] implemented a Bayesian
model approach for single station and group model prediction for multiple stations in the danish
coastal waters, showing that water temperature, nutrient loading, salinity and water columns
stability were the most abundant predictors. Furthermore, there has been a lot of research in
the utilization of more complex machine learning models, including multi­layer neural networks
and random forests for single point CHL prediction, for instance [100], [101] and [102].
The downside of all these methods is the ignorance of any spatial dynamics. As mentioned in
the previous section of gap­filling, in order to face this problem there has been a lot of research
in using satellite observations to capture the spatial dynamics. Higher resolution and ever more
satellites have led to many studies that use machine learning models to extract and predict CHL
concentrations from remote sensing data. Su et al. [103] implemented a new Gradient Boosting
machine learning algorithm applied on satellite data. Their model shows good results for the
research area of the Fujian coastline with a high correlation in comparison to the in­situ data that
was used for the evaluation. Choi et al. [104] implemented a CNN model for CHL prediction in
South Korea (Daechung Lake) with a focus on data handling, showing that log­transformation
of the data before training can help the model to learn rare­event occurances. Cen et al. [14]
implement a LSTM model to predict CHL concentration in the South China Sea, with the focus
on finding optimal data­transformation for the model input. They show that the original data as
input to the model shows an increased performance in comparison to log transformed data. Na
et al. [16] compare the performance of LSTM and a combination of CNN and LSTM models
and show, that combining CNN and LSTM models can improve the model performance.
While the methods that use single­variable data show promising results, some studies have
shown that incorporating other features might yield more accurate predictions and thus it is
beneficial to also include oceanographic and atmospheric parameters into the training process
of the model (Cen et al. [14], Yussof et al. [105]). Jin et al. [106] introduced a CNN model
for plankton prediction in a South Korean marine area, that combines the output of numerical
models and remote sensing ocean color data as the input to the model. To overcome the prob­
lem of insufficient training data, they split the data spatially into smaller areas that were used
for training the model, which resulted in higher accuracy. Similar to Na et al. [16], Barzegar et
al. [107] also implements a hybrid CNN ­ LSTM model, yet including also other relevant factors
into the model training, such as pH and water temperature. Zhang et al. [15] implement a Con­
vLSTM Model to predict CHL concentrations, as well as multiple other water quality variables in
Dongping Lake, based on features extracted from Landsat 8 imagery. Zhou et al. [108] imple­
ment a stacked ConvLSTM model for wave forcasting that shows more accurate results than
the compatible numerical model. The difference between ConvLSTM models and CNN­LSTM
models is that in CNN­LSTM models the data is commonly first convoluted and then flattened
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
21
==PAGE:40==
to be passed to the LSTM model, while in an ConvLSTM model, the convolution is already part
of the recursive model.
While the above mentioned methods focus either on the gap­filling or the prediction of plankton
populations, none of the publications addresses both issues at once. Only very recently, in
March 2023, Ye et al. [109] published a paper on the prediction of plankton population with
gap­filled remote sensing data. They successfully use the above­mentioned DINEOF method
to fill the gaps in the CHL data from the ESA ocean colour dataset for the Bohai Sea in China
and use this gap­filled data for further predictions. For the predictions a new hybrid CNN­LSTM
model was introduced, the Ca­STANet. The model differs from the previously mentioned models
by chunking the area of research into smaller sub­areas that are passed to individual CNN
and LSTM models, while also calculating a global convolution in order to also mine the spatial
correlation between sub­areas. For the output, the sub­areas are then patched together for the
final result. The LSTM part of the model uses multi­head attention, as introduced by Vasvani
et al. [110], allowing the model to attend to multiple parts of the input sequence simultaneously
and thus improve the feature extraction capacity.
Many approaches have already been explored to predict CHL concentrations, especially in
Asian waters. Given the ability of ConvLSTM models to integrate spatial and temporal depen­
dencies simultaneously, this methods is chosen as one of the prediction models in this study.
Furthermore, Lin et al. [111] has introduced a ConvLSTM model with self­attention that has
shown superior results compared to common ConvLSTM models on the famous MovingMNIST
dataset [112]. Additionally, Ye et al. [109] have shown that attention based models can achieve
good performances for CHL prediction. This study is therefore utilizing those two methods to
perform the prediction of CHL concentration in the Baltic Sea.
22
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:41==
4
Data
The most important data that is required for the project is the CHL concentration from remote
sensing data. Additionally, multiple features are chosen to support the predictions, based on
domain knowledge from experts within the company DHI as well as previous studies that have
shown a high correlation between external factors, weather phenomena and CHL concentra­
tions in the Baltic Sea as well as the Mediterranean Sea ([11], [113], [114]). The majority of the
data originates from a combination of level 2, level 3 and level 4 remote sensing data of multiple
polar­orbiting satellites with passive sensors and varying temporal and spatial resolutions, the
theory of which is explained in Section 2.4. Yet also numerical and in­situ data is incorporated
and used as baseline and ground­truth for model comparison. The project is focussed on the
Baltic Sea area, using three predefined subareas of the whole sea to train and evaluate the
different models.
In the following, the research area is presented and explored and the data origin and methods
that were used to extract the data are introduced. Furthermore, a small data exploration is
performed for data selection and the preprocessing steps to uniformize the data are outlined.
4.1
Research Area
The general focus area of the project is the Baltic Sea. As representatives of the whole sea,
three subareas are chosen. These are an area in the Southern Kattegat, an area in the Gotland
Basin and the Gulf of Riga as shown in Figure 4.1. The Gulf of Riga is chosen as the main focus
area to conduct experiences on model architecture, with the other areas to validate the model
performances representative for the whole area of the Baltic Sea.
Figure 4.1: Research area, showing the three subareas in the Baltic Sea: Southern Kattegat, Gulf of Riga and
Gotland Basin, Google Maps, 2023 [115]
The choice of the specific subareas is made because the three areas capture different character­
istics of the Baltic Sea. The main geographical conditions that influence the plankton population
abundance are the topography and the closeness to coastal areas. As explained in Section 2.1,
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
23
==PAGE:42==
these two factors can have a significant influence on plankton dynamics. In order to understand
the differences between the three areas and motivate the choice of these three areas to repre­
sent the entire Baltic Sea, the depth of the Baltic Sea, and the distance between each pixel that
is classified as an ocean pixel to the closest land pixel are plotted in Figure 4.2 and 4.3.
Figure 4.2: Depth of the Baltic Sea, zoomed in to the three subareas: Southern Kattegat, Gulf of Riga and Gotland
Basin
Figure 4.3: Distance of each ocean pixel to the closest land pixel for the Baltic Sea, zoomed in to the three subareas:
Southern Kattegat, Gulf of Riga and Gotland Basin
Based on these figures, the main difference can be observed between the Gotland Basin and
the other two areas. While the Gulf of Riga and the Southern Kattegat are coastal, shallow
areas with a maximal depth of 65m and the furthest distance from land of 42km, the part of the
24
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:43==
Gotland Basin that is selected for the project, represents open and deeper waters with a depth
of up to 152m and a distance from land of up to 121km. However, the Kattegat lies in the in­flow
area of water from the North Sea, which leads to a higher mixing rate of the water and makes the
area richer in oxygen, differentiating it from the very enclosed Gulf of Riga. Therefore, the three
areas provide a good representation of the whole Baltic Sea by providing information about
coastal as well as open water, and oxygen rich as well as oxygen poor areas.
The Gulf of Riga is one of the most eutrophic areas in the Baltic Sea, due to its enclosure and
low mixing rate [116]. Therefore, it it is chosen as the main area for the experiments, assuming
that if the models perform well in the Gulf of Riga, they will also perform well in less complex
waters.
4.2
Data Origin
The project is based on open­source data from the Copernicus Marine Service as well as the Eu­
ropean Organisation for the Exploitation of Meteorological Satellites (EUMETSAT), using data
from multiple data catalogues and satellite missions. The data from the Copernicus Marine
Service is accessed via the motuclient API [117], a webserver provided to handle and extract
large volumes of oceanographic data. The OLCI L2 data from EUMETSAT is accessed via
the EUMETSAT Data Access Client (EUMDAC) API [118], a datastore that allows access to
satellite data of various missions. To extract the CHL information from the L2 data, the data
is first reprojected to an evenly spaced grid, before being converted to the needed spatial
and temporal resolution. The meteorological information and the in­situ measurements are
downloaded manually from the Copernicus Climate Data Store and the Copernicus Marine
Service respectively.
An overview of the access methods of all datasets together with the
product ids is provided in Table 4.1. The code for all data extractions is available on github:
https://github.com/NinaOeh/Masterthesis_Code.
4.2.1
Chlorophyll­a Concentration
The CHL data is the main data used in this project, as it builds the foundation for the gap
filling and prediction models. In order to compare the recent numerical model outputs to the
model predictions of the project, very recent data is needed. However, there is no dataset
that spans until today and has a long time reach into the past. Therefore, to utilize as much
training data for the models as possible, data from three different sources is compared and a
combination of two datasets is selected as the data for the CHL concentration. The first dataset
will be referred to as MULTI and is the level 3 Baltic Sea Multiyear Ocean Colour Plankton,
Reflectances and Transparency L3 daily observations [119] product with a spatial resolution
of 1km from the Copernicus Marine Service. Is has a daily resolution for a time­series from
September 1997 to December 2021.
The second dataset will be referred to as OLCI, which stands for Ocean and Land Color Instru­
ment and is part of the Sentinel 3 mission. It spans from 2016 until today, and provides near real
time daily data, thanks to the two satellites Sentinel 3A and Sentinel 3B, that orbit earth. It has
a high spatial resolution of 300m, yet the quality of the OLCI data is lower and it has more noise
compared to the MULTI dataset. To choose the most appropriate dataset, the OLCI L3 dataset
that is provided by the Copernicus Marine Service and the OLCI L2 dataset that is provided by
EUMETSAT are exported and compared to each other, as well as the MULTI data.
The target variable CHL concentration [ mg
m3 ] can be determined by analyzing the reflectance of
the sea surface. This reflectance is calculated using top­of­atmosphere radiance, which has
been processed with atmospheric correction algorithms to remove any interference caused by
atmospheric aerosols, water surface glint, whitecaps, nearby land, clouds, snow, or ice. The
quality of the CHL estimation depends largely on the performance of the atmospheric correc­

tion algorithms to extract the most accurate remote sensing reflectance (Rrs) [129]. Given its
shallowness and brackish waters, the Baltic Sea is highly optical complex and considered a
challenging water for remote sensing analysis [130], [131], leading to poor results when using
standard ocean­color­retrieval algorithms that are based on blue–green band­ratio polynomial
regression [132].
MULTI and OLCI L3
To face this problem, Brando et al, [43] implemented an ensemble multilayer perceptron neural
net (ENS­MLP) bio­optical algorithm, that is implemented by the Copernicus Marine Service
to generate the Ocean Color L3 products of the Baltic Sea. To generate the MULTI dataset,
sensor data from multiple satellites (MERIS/ENVISAT, MODIS/AQUA, SeaWiFS/SEASTAR,
VIIRS/SUOMI­NPP and OLCI) is utilized with two different atmospheric correction algorithms,
based on which algorithm performs best for which sensor. To generate the OLCI L3 dataset,
the Polymer atmospheric correction algorithm, that is known to show good results for coastal
waters [133], is used on the OLCI L1 data of the Sentinel 3 mission to extract the remote sensing
reflections Rrs. Based on these reflections, the ENS­MLP algorithm, as explained in Appendix
A.1.1 is then applied to extract the CHL concentrations.
26
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:45==
OLCI L2
To generate the OLCI L2 dataset, a different approach than that to generate the L3 data is
used by EUMETSAT. The CHL concentration is based on the alternative atmospheric correction
algorithm applied on the OLCI L1 data of the Sentinel 3 mission. This method is an updated
version of the case 2 coastal water algorithm, that was initially created by Doerffer et al. [134] for
optically more complex case 2 waters, where the optical properties of the water can be explained
by more than only one property (CHL). The algorithm to extract the CHL concentration for the
generation of the OLCI L2 data is appended in Appendix A.1.2.
4.2.2
Sea Surface Temperature (SST)
The Sea Surface Temperature (SST) is a level 4 remote sensing product provided by the Coper­
nicus Marine Service and generated by the Danish Meterological Institute (DMI). The data has
a temporal daily resolution and a spatial resolution of approximately 2km. It originates from
the night time SST measurements level 2 data of multiple satellites (infrared data from NOAA,
MetOp­A, MSG, Suomi­NPP and Sentinel 3A satellites), calculated with the Operational Sea
surface Temperature and Ice Analysis (DMIOI) system and validated with in­situ measurements.
To generate the level 4 product, the data is interpolated using optimal interpolation (Høyer et
al. [135]) to fill missing values. The exported dataset with the target variable SST [K] provides
data for the time span of 2016 until today. [136]
4.2.3
Wave Data
The wave data is generated with numerical wave models, providing hourly data of multiple wave
parameters with a spatial resolution of about 1.85km and a temporal hourly resolution. For the
scope of the project the spectral significant wave height (VHM0) [m] and mean sea surface
wave from direction (VMDR) [◦] are utilized.
The data originates from two datasets, one ranging from 1993 to 2021 and the other one ranging
from 2019 to today. The newer dataset is used in its full availability and concatenated to the older
dataset for the remaining years between 2016 and 2019 to provide the wave information for the
project. Both datasets are based on the wave model WAM cycle 4.6.2. The WAM cycle 4.6.2
is an improved version of the WAM cycle 4 wave model (Guenther et al. [137]), that solves the
spectral action balance without any preassumptions regarding the shape of the wave spectrum.
The older dataset is a hindsight product that includes meterological forcing from the ECMWF
ERA5 reanalysis product [138], while the newer dataset is an analysis and forecast product that
includes forcing from the CMEMS BAL MFC ocean forecast product [139]. [140] [141]
4.2.4
Atmospheric Data
The atmospheric data is the reanalysis product ERA5 of the Copernicus Climate Data Store.
ERA5 is the fifth generation of the European Centre for Medium­Range Weather Forecasts
(ECMWF) providing a multitude of weather and atmosphere data with a spatial resolution of
25km and an hourly temporal resolution. The parameters chosen to be integrated in the project
are the total prescipitation (tp) [m], air temperature (t2m) [K], eastward component of wind
(u10) [ m
s ] and northward component of wind (v10) [ m
s ] as well as total sky direct solar radiation
at surface [ J
m2 ]. In the reanalysis, in­situ measurements are assimilated into the numerical
model output to generate more precise parameter estimations. [125]
4.2.5
Static Data
The static data includes the topography and land mask of the Baltic Sea. The data has a
spatial resolution of 2km and is exported from the Leipzig Institute for Baltic Sea Research
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
27
==PAGE:46==
Warnemünde [126]. While this information is provided in many of the other datasets, it was
decided to use an external dataset for these variables to ensure their independence.
4.2.6
Biogeochemical Model Output
The biogeochemical model output that is used for model validation is the Baltic Sea Biogeo­
chemistry Analysis and Forecast product of the Copernicus Marine Service [124]. The model
is the biogeochemical model ERGOM by Neumann et al.[94], coupled with the physical NEMO
model by Madec et al. [95]. The model builds on the fundamentals as described in Section
2.2, yet is more complex than the model described in this section. It provides data for a multi­
tude of biochemical variables such as nitrate, phosphate and CHL. To ensure independence of
the numerical model outputs and the deep learning model outputs, this model is only used for
validation and only the CHL parameter is exported. The data has a spatial resolution of approx­
imately 2km and an hourly temporal resolution. It provides data in up to 56 vertical depths, yet
only the smallest depth that provides information at a depth closest to the seasurface at 0.5m is
used for the comparison. The forecast data is downloaded daily in the time period of 20.06.2023
and 25.06.2023. Initially the month of May was supposed to be the validation month and the
numerical predictions where continuously downloaded during that month. However, unfortu­
nately, due to a technical error in the Copernicus Marine Service, no OLCI data was available
for that month, which was discovered only on the 20th of June. Thereafter, the forecast data
was downloaded daily in the duration from the 20.06.2023 to 25.06.2023 to be used for the
model comparison.
It must be noted that the biogeochemical model is known to underestimate the magnitude of
the CHL concentration [142].
4.2.7
In­Situ Data
The in­situ samples are taken frequently in multiple fixed measurement stations across the Baltic
Sea as well as on board of vessels and ferries, as they cross the sea. The data is managed by
the Swedish Meteorological and Hydrological Institute (SMHI) and distributed via the Copernicus
Marine Service [127].
The two parameters total CHL [ mg
m3 ] and CHL flourescence [ mg
m3 ] are exported. The prior parame­
ter of total CHL is commonly extracted using a spectrophotometric method [143], following ISO
10260. To do so, the water sample is vacuum filtered, steeped in ethanol and clarified through
centrifugation. The absorbance of the clarified extract is then measured on a spectrophotome­
ter and the CHL concentration calculated based on the volume of ethanol, absorption coefficient
in ethanol, filtered water volume and length of cuvette.
The CHL flourescence is extracted using an optical method in which the flourescene emission of
the algae is measured. When light shines on the plants, they absorb photons. This absorption
elevates electrons in CHL molecules to an excited state. Yet this state is highly unsteady and
thus the electron immediately returns to the ground state, whereby it emits energy in form of a
fluorescence photon. This energy can be measured and reveal the extend of CHL fluorescence
in the sample [144]. Each instrument must be carefully calibrated and measurements corrected
to the given circumstances according to the HELCOM guidelines [143].
Due to the before mentioned technical problems, the downloaded time period spans between
20.06.2023 to 28.06.2023, in order to compare the predictions of the model that will be estab­
lished in this thesis project and the bio­geochemical model with the in­situ measurements. At
the time where the data was accessed, which was the 30.06.2023, unfortunately only ferrybox
data of CHL flourescence in a small area of the Gotland Basin was available from the Coperni­
cus Marine Service, collected by the ferry ”Finnmaid”. Figure 4.4 shows the route of the ferry
28
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:47==
with the individual points where samples were taken.
Figure 4.4: Ferrybox measurement availability between 20.06.2023 and 28.06.2023 (as of 30.06.2023)
4.3
Data Exploration
In order to gain a better understanding of the available data, a small visual exploration of the
datasets is undertaken. Given the large number of datasets, not all datasets are thoroughly
explored, but the focus is laid on the CHL datasets. An overview of the distribution of the
additional features is attached in Appendix A.1.5.
To ensure the best possible data quality, a choice must be made as to which CHL datasets to
use. This is done by comparing the OLCI L2, OLCI L3 and MULTI datasets to known annual
patterns of plankton blooms as well as to each other. Once this decision is made, the general
data distribution and availability in the three defined research areas is explored.
4.3.1
Comparison of OLCI L2, OLCI L3 and MULTI Data
To compare the CHL datasets, the year 2021 is chosen to conduct the comparison, as it is
present in all three datasets. Due to the large size of the datasets, when comparing them on
a pixel by pixel value, statistical tests are considered non­suitable. Instead, visual inspections
and effect­size estimations are used.
Figure 4.5 shows the spatial mean and standard deviation for the three datasets. The figure
illustrates that the OLCI L2 dataset has substantially larger values than the other datasets, with
a mean of 7.18 mg
m3 , compared to the mean value of 2.77 mg
m3 for the MULTI dataset and the
mean value of 4.45 mg
m3 for the OLCI L3 dataset. Additionally it does not seem to follow the
same patterns, for instance it does not necessarily show larger values in brackish areas like
the Gulf of Riga. Overall, all datasets show a large fluctuation, relative to the mean value, with
standard deviations reaching from 3.04 mg
m3 for the MULTI dataset, over 5.68 mg
m3 for the OLCI
L3 dataset to 8.55 mg
m3 for the OLCI L2 dataset. As expected from previous studies of plankton
population dynamics, as presented in Section 2.1, the MULTI and OLCI L3 datasets show a

Due to these unexpected variabilities, the Copernicus Marine Service is contacted and asked,
how the differences between the datasets can be explained. The main reason that is provided
for the deviation between the MULTI and OLCI L3 dataset are dissimilarities in the sensors
that cause different Rrs values in the bands that are used for the calculation. For the deviation
between the OLCI L2 and OLCI L3 dataset, the different atmospheric correction algorithms that
are used to calculate the CHL concentration in the two data products, based on the OLCI level 1
data, are provided as cause for the observed differences. The full correspondance is attached
in Appendix A.1.4.
In order to find the most accurate data, the annual trends of all three datasets are compared to
each other and to known annual trends of plankton population dynamics, namely the timing of
the spring and summer bloom in the Baltic Sea, as described in Section 2.1.
Thus, it is expected to observe an increase in CHL during the spring bloom that starts around the
end of March and lasts for approximately 3 month and the summer bloom that starts around the
beginning of June and lasts for approximately 2.5 month. Figure 4.6 shows an interpolated time
series of the CHL concentration for the three datasets in the three different subareas, smoothed
by taking the rolling average over 10 days with time periods of expected blooms shaded in grey.
The figure shows that all three datasets do not fully capture the bloom periods and especially
in winter the overall concentration seems to be overestimated. However, the MULTI and OLCI
L3 datasets show higher values compared to the rest of the year during the expected bloom
periods than the OLCI L2 dataset, that highly overestimates the concentration towards the start
and the end of the year. Furthermore, it can be seen that while the MULTI and OLCI L3 dataset
follow a similiar trend, the OLCI L2 dataset deviates strongly from that trend, as the calculated
R2 value already suggested.
Taking all presented aspects into account, the OLCI L2 dataset was dropped from the further
process and the analysis proceeds solely with the MULTI and OLCI L3 data, which will be called
OLCI in the remaining report for simplicity.

4.3.2
Chlorophyll­A Data Availability and Distribution
In a next step the CHL data availability and distribution in the three subareas for the two chosen
datasets is analysed. Taking the average percentage for the whole time­span of each dataset
individually, in the Gulf of Riga the percentage of available data is 24.98% for the MULTI dataset
and 21.56% for the OLCI dataset, in the southern Kattegat it is 30.83% for the MULTI dataset
and 20.50% for the OLCI dataset and in the souther­eastern Gotland Basin 30.20% for the
MULTI dataset and 27.61% for the OLCI dataset. The difference between the two datasets can
most likely be attributed to a lower accuracy in the sensor data when classifying pixels either
as water or land data, which is most uncertain in shallow waters where more sandbanks are
present, such as the Southern Kattegat.
When taking a closer look at how much data is available, as shown in Figure 4.7, one can see
that most pixels are available during the middle of the year, in summer, for all subareas and there
is a large gap during the winter months. Apart from the weather being more cloudy during winter,
this observation can be explained by the high solar zenit angle of the sun during these months
32
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:51==
in the higher latitude that the Baltic Sea lies in. The common atmospheric correction algorithms
fail to calculate the reflection appropriately and thus no data for these month can be generated
[145]. This gap during winter also partly accounts for the very low average data availability.
Leaving out the months of January, November and December, the average data availability
rises to 33,54% and 26.62%, 40.84% and 27.05%, 39.73% and 36.64% for the MULTI and
OLCI dataset in the Gulf of Riga, Kattegat and Gotland Basin respectively.
Figure 4.7: Time series of pixel availablity for both datasets in all three subareas
Furthermore, it can be observed that more pixels are available in the Gotland Basin than in
the Southern Kattegat and in the Gulf of Riga. This is simply explained by the choice of the
patch that includes the subarea. Subsequently, the patch of the Gulf of Riga includes more
land pixels than in the Southern Kattegat and in the Gotland Basin. Overall the pixel availability
increases over time. A likely cause for this in the MULTI dataset is the start of more satellite
missions in that time range that were integrated into the data product. For the OLCI dataset,
this observation can be explained with the launch of the Sentinel 3B satellite in April 2018 that
complements the data of the Sentinel 3A satellite that was launched already in 2016.
Figure 4.8: Data distribution for both datasets in all three subareas
Looking at the data distribution on a per­pixel basis in all three areas, as shown in Figure 4.8,
one can see that the two datasets show slightly different distributions. Especially, the OLCI
dataset has a higher occurrence of small values than the MULTI dataset, while also reaching
higher concentration levels. Most of those fluctuations can be explained by noise in the data.
Yet when doing a preliminary visual inspection, large patches of unnaturally low values in the
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
33
==PAGE:52==
Gulf of Riga area are noticed, that can not be explained by the provided quality index, as shown
in Figure 4.9. This behaviour can however only be observed in the Gulf of Riga and not in the
other datasets, and does not occur at all in the MULTI dataset. Given the limited time frame
of the project, the reason for the occurrence of these patches is not further investigated and a
work around is found by masking out values that are smaller than 1 for the area of the Gulf of
Riga. Furthermore, the quality index is disregarded, given the low data availability overall and
that no obvious reason for masking the flagged pixels can be identified.

4.4
Data Preprocessing
In preparation for the model import, it is essential to convert all data to a uniform temporal (daily)
and spatial (1km) resolution. To establish the temporal resolution, the data is first accumulated
into daily averages. Subsequently, missing days within the datasets are identified and filled
with NaN values. To address the spatial resolution the interp_like function from the xarray
library [146] is employed, that linearly interpolates one array to the same spatial resolution
as another array. This process ensures that all data aligns with the required temporal and
spatial resolutions. Due to the non­linear behaviour and complexity of deep learning models,
transforming the distribution of all features to Gaussian distributions is not necessary. Instead,
in this project the original distribution of the features is maintained, allowing the model to identify
the underlying structures and relationships within the data. By using level 4 remote sensing and
numerical data, it is ensured that no gaps are present in the additional feature data. Given that
the overall aim of monitoring plankton populations is to predict and capture blooming events,
the prediction of plankton blooms can to a certain extend be considered an extreme event
forecast, as the blooms only happen during a relatively short time period within each year.
Outliers are therefore kept in the datasets, to enable the model to capture the underlying patterns
that possibly cause those blooming events.
The gap­filling and prediction models are provided with different data and require varying data
preprocessing steps. Those are explained in detail for each model in Chapter 6. The gap­
filling models are trained solely on the CHL concentration datasets, including possible features
that can be extracted from these datasets, such as the day of year and latitude and longitude.
Meanwhile, the prediction models are trained on all available features and used to analyse the
feature importance in the last step. The output of the prediction model is then compared to the
numerical model output as well as the in­situ samples. An overview of the data composition for
gap­filling, prediction and evaluation of the models is provided in Appendix A.1.6.
34
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:53==
5
Model Architectures
To address the research questions, multiple state­of­the­art deep learning models for gap­filling
and prediction are selected, based on extensive literature review as provided in Chapter 3.
These models are optimized in several steps through different experiments and the best per­
forming model is chosen to be applied on all areas and for the final comparison to numerical
and in­situ data. Futhermore, a feature importance analysis is conducted based on the best
performing prediction model.
In the project, the ADAM optimizer, as explained in Section 2.3.1 will be used for all models,
as it has shown superior performance in numerous studies and increases the speed of model
convergence. Moreover, as both negative and positive values larger than 1 shall be predicted,
a simple linear activation function as explained in Section 2.3.2 is used in all output layers. All
models were set up with the open­source python library keras [147]. The keras library offers a
diverse range of pre­defined layers, activation functions and optimization algorithms, while also
allowing to integrate customized classes. The code for this thesis, including all models, can be
accessed on github https://github.com/NinaOeh/Masterthesis_Code.
The following section explains the setup and design choices for the gap­filling models and the
predictions models, based on the papers that introduce the initial model architectures.
5.1
Gap­Filling
In order to address the first research question and given the sparcity of the available data, the
DINCAE model by Barth et al. [88] and the STpCONV3D model by Appel et al. [82] were
modified, resulting in three models that were compared in order to find a suitable model setup
to fill the gaps in the data. The quality of the gap­filling is crucial for the consecutive prediction,
as the predictions will be made based on the gap­filled data.
The problem of gap­filling can be considered an image reconstruction task, as the model is sup­
posed to learn to reconstruct the given image, yet adding some more information to it, to fill the
gaps. As described in Section 2.3.5, autoencoders are powerful models for image reconstruc­
tion and have been used in a variety of image reconstruction applications. Thus, a common
design choice that all three models share is that they are autoencoders. However, while the
general setup of these models is always the same, there are many modifications possible.
5.1.1
DINCAE and DINpCAE Model
The DINCAE model was introduced by Barth et al. [88] to effectively fill gaps in spatio­temporal
data of sea surface temperature and has proven successful to fill gaps in spatio­temporal CHL
data by Han et al. [87].
For the scope of this project, the source code of the model was translated to tensorflow keras v.2
using the openly available Julia code by Barth et al. that is available on https://github.com/gher-
uliege/DINCAE.jl and the python version of DINCAEv.1 that is available on https://github.com/
gher-uliege/DINCAE for reference.
In an attempt to adjust the model architecture to handle the very sparse data provided in this
project, the DINCAE model was extended and the method of partial convolutions by Liu et al.
[83] was integrated into the model to create a new Data INterpolating partially Convolutional
AutoEncoder (DINpCAE) model.
Both models are setup of a refined autoencoder, that is composed of encoder and decoder.
While the original DINCAE model uses the same architecture for both sequential U­Nets, the
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
35
==PAGE:54==
first U­Net of DINpCAE uses a series of partial convolutions and the second U­Net implements
normal convolutions. The architecture of both models is visualized in Figure 5.1, indicating the
input of mask and data in the first U­Net and only the data in the second U­Net for the DINpCAE
model, as well as the skip connections between the en­ and decoder.
Partial Convolutions
The partial convolutions are integrated into the DINpCAE model, in order to focus the model only
on the non­gap areas at each convolutional layer and thus create output only based on valid
inputs. As defined by Liu et al. [83] this is achieved by passing a binary mask along through
the network, that is updated after every layer when the convolution was able to fill a gap with
information. The partial convolution in every location can be defined as:
x′ =

Where W are the filter weights for the convolution, X are the pixel values for the current convo­
lution, b is the bias, M is the binary mask and N is the total number of pixels. The filter weights
are element­wise multiplied with the pixel values and scaled by the amount of valid pixels as
indicated by the total­pixels to valid­pixels­in­binary­mask ratio. If the current convolution was
able to base the output on at least one valid input value, the mask is updated by:
M′ =

(5.2)
This way, when enough consecutive layers are present, every mask can be modified to consist
only of ones and thus all gaps of the image can be filled.
Skip­Connections
Skip connections are implemented as a measure to avoid the vanishing gradient problem. While
the initial U­Net architecture [33] utilized concatenating skip­connections, the DINCAE model
and DINpCAE model use sum­skip­connections, adding together the corresponding layers of
the encoder and decoder, similarly to the implementation of the famous ResNet by He at al.
[80], where the output of previous layers is added to the output of consecutive layers to form
the input of following layers and thereby reducing the vanishing gradient problem.
Pooling
While a classical U­Net architecture consists of a combination of convolutions and pooling,
recent studies have shown that the pooling layer can be left out and the kernel in addition to a
suitable stride can be used instead, as explained in Section 2.3. Thus, in the DINpCAE model
a stride size of 2 is used for dimensionality reduction.
Refinement
The second U­Net is implemented to refine the single U­Net in order to make the network deeper
and more accurate. To overcome the vanishing gradient problem, the intermediate results are
included in the loss function. Therefore, during back­propagation through the loss function,
information of the observation is not only injected in the last but also in the intermediate layer
and therefore more direct to the input. The input to the second U­Net is the output of the first
U­Net concatenated with the initial input.
36
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:55==

L(ˆyn, ˆσ2

second U­Net, where α(U1) and α(U2) are the ratios that defines how much importance to give
to the intermediate (U1) and final (U2) U­net respectively.

5.1.2
pCONV3D
The pCONV3D model is based on the STpConv model by Appel [82]. The source code of
the model is available on https://github.com/appelmar/STpconv and the code structure is kept
in the presented project, yet includes some variations to the original model setup to suit the
application. For example ’.nc’ files are utilized instead of ’.tiff’ files for model input, additional
channels are added for the dataset information and the model architecture is modified. The
model has the structure of a U­Net with an encoder and a decoder, using three dimensional
partial convolutions. Instead of just two layers for en­ and decoding as is done in the STpConv
model, the pCONV3D model uses four layers, as visualized in Section 2.3, making the model
deeper and thus capable to find more complex structures.
Partial Convolutions
As in the initial implementation, all convolutions in the model are defined to be partial con­
volutions, thus focusing the attention of the network only on the available pixels. The partial
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
37
==PAGE:56==
convolution is shown in Equation (5.1), calculating the dot product between the binary mask
and the pixel values of the current convolution relative to all known pixels. Thereafter the binary
mask is updated as shown in Equation (5.2), aiming to fill the mask until only ones and therefore
known values are present.
Skip­Connections
Following Appel [82], the skip connections for the pCONV3D model are the same as in the inital
U­Net [33], thus the output of the encoder layer is concatenated to the output of the decoder
layer of the same level within the U­Net.
Pooling/ Stride
For dimensionality reduction, instead of using pooling layers, the stride size is increased. Given
the irregular size of the input tensor, that is not the same in all dimensions, the stride is de­
fined to be [(2, 2, 2),(2, 2, 1),(2, 2, 1), (2, 2, 2)] for the four layers respectively, reducing the
dimensionality in the spatial dimension more than in the time dimension.
Regularization
While the original STpConv model does not include regularization techniques, batch normaliza­
tion is added to the pCONV3D model. While this method helps to avoid overfitting of the model
to the training data, it can also help to fasten the model convergence. In the pCONV3D model,
batch normalization is applied after every convolutional layer.
Loss Function
The loss function for the pCONV3D model is the same as in the STpConv model, yet applied
only on the validation data. Thus it is the mean average error (MAE) between the observed and
the predicted validation pixels.
L(x, y) =
PN
i=1 |xi −yi|
N
(5.7)
Where N is the total number of validation pixels, x is the observed value and y is the predicted
value.

Appendix A.2.

5.2
Prediction
The models for the prediction part of the project are built upon the ConvLSTM architecture pro­
posed by Shi et al. [36]. As described in Section 5.2.2, the model is designed to capture spatial
and temporal dependencies in sequential data by integrating convolutions into the recursive
LSTM model. This ability is important in order to effectively capture the dynamic behavior of
38
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:57==
phytoplankton, which involves analyzing data with simultaneous spatial and temporal character­
istics. For the project specifically, two models are compared: a stacked ConvLSTM model and
a stacked ConvLSTM model with Self­Attention. These models have a simplified architectures
compared to the gap­filling models, consisting of multiple stacked layers.
When dealing with multi­step predictions, two viable approaches can be considered. The recur­
sive model involves iteratively predicting one step at a time, using previous predictions as input
for generating subsequent predictions. On the other hand, the multiple output strategy aims to
directly predict multiple future steps simultaneously. Previous studies by Wei et al. [149] on
predicting sea surface temperature using a ConvLSTM model have demonstrated that the mul­
tiple output strategy yields more robust results for the predictions. Taking that into account and
considering the objective of including multiple features and the uncertainty of having advance
knowledge of these additional features, the multiple output strategy is chosen for the project.
Therefore, the models are configured to predict multiple days ahead at once, facilitating the
incorporation of various features and enabling a comprehensive analysis of the target variable.
5.2.1
ConvLSTM Model
The stacked ConvLSTM model consists of three stacked ConvLSTM layers that extract temporal
features from the input data using the recursive LSTM part of the model while capturing spatial
dependencies through convolutions. The output layer is a 3D convolutional layer that takes the
output of the previous ConvLSTM layers and applies a 3D convolution to generate the model
output. The number of filters in the final convolution corresponds to the number of days that are
predicted ahead. An alternative setup that includes a final convolution for each day individually
was preliminary implemented, yet discarded due to big fluctuation in the model performance as
the model would get stuck in local minima instead of optimizing the weights in order to predict
all days accordingly. A small analysis of the setup is attached in Appendix A.4.2. Figure 5.3
shows the final setup of the ConvLSTM model. Each ConvLSTM layer takes a sequence of
days as input and also returns a sequence of outputs that are passed to the next ConvLSTM
layer. To make the predictions, only the last output of the final ConvLSTM layer is passed to the
Conv3D layer, after applying batch normalization.
Figure 5.3: ConvLSTM model Architecture
Regularization
To avoid overfitting of the model to the training dataset and speed up the model convergence,
batch normalization is added to the model after the last ConvLSTM layer. This techniques helps
to generalize the model’s performance and improves its ability to generalize well to unseen data.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
39
==PAGE:58==
Loss Function
The loss function for the ConvLSTM model is the mean squared error loss function.
L(x, y) =
PN
i=1(xi −yi)2
N
(5.8)
Where N is the total number of pixels, x is the observed value and y is the predicted value.
5.2.2
ConvLSTM with Self­Attention Model
The stacked ConvLSTM with Self­Attention (Sa­ConvLSTM) model architecture was introduced
by Lin et al. [111] to solve the problem of ConvLSTM models to cover long range spatial de­
pendencies. The project utilizes the publicly available Sa­ConvLSTM class [150] to set up the
Sa­ConvLSTM model that consists of three stacked Sa­ConvLSTM layers and a final 3D con­
volutional layer. As visualized in Figure 5.4, the architecture of the model is the same as for
the ConvLSTM model, yet using the Sa­ConvLSTM class for the recursive layers instead of the
ConvLSTM class.
Figure 5.4: Sa­ConvLSTM model Architecture
Self Attention ConvLSTM Module
The attention mechanism was first introduced by Vaswani et al. [110] and later integrated into
the ConvLSTM layer by Lin et al. [111] to capture larger scale spatial dependencies between
days. In the implementation of Lin et al. [111] , that is used in the project, the Self­Attention
mechanism is directly integrated into the ConvLSTM layer, by adding a new memory unit into
the cell. The output of this memory unit is passed on as part of the recursion, as illustrated
with m in Figure 5.4. The inside of the memory unit can be split into three parts, a feature
aggregation part, a memory updating part and the output. The input to the unit are the outputs
of the ConvLSTM part of the current cell, ˆht and the output of the memory unit of the previous
cell, mt−1. In the memory unit, the total aggregated features Z are calculated by combining
the aggregated features of the two inputs Zˆh and Zm. To calculate Zˆh the self­attention method
works by splitting the original feature maps Ht into three different feature spaces:
the query: Qˆh = Wh,qˆht
(5.9)
the key: Kˆh = Wˆh,kˆht
(5.10)
the value: Vˆh = Wˆh,vHt
(5.11)
with Wˆh,q, Wˆh,k and Wˆh,v as a set of weights for 1x1 convolutions. To calculate the similarity
scores for each pixel pair, matrix multiplication is applied:
eˆh,i,j = QT

(5.12)

Regularization
Same as the ConvLSTM model, the Sa­ConvLSTM model applies batch normalization to help
the model generalize also to unseen datasets and thus avoid overfitting. Furthermore, this
method also helps to speed up the model convergence.
