6
Experiments and Results
The aim of this study is to investigate if deep learning models are practicable for filling spatio­
temporal gaps in remote sensing data of CHL concentrations and a suitable alternative or sup­
plement to the current numerical model for plankton predictions. Furthermore, the goal is to
identify which features are most important for the predictions.
Therefore, appropriate deep learning models must be found to fit the given problems. As previ­
ously described in Chapter 5, different models are identified from literature review to be promis­
ing candidates for this task. The following chapter outlines the experiments that were conducted
to find the best model setup and choose one most appropriate model for both the gap­filling and
then, based on the result of the gap­filling, for the prediction. Finally, the output of the chosen
model is compared to naive interpolation and baseline methods, as well as the numerical model.
The experiments are presented chronologically, with the following experiment being conducted
based on the best result of the previous experiment. Figure 6.1 shows an overview in form of
a flow chart of all experiments that are performed, as well as their chronological position in the
process, including the feature analysis.
Figure 6.1: Overview and flowchart of all experiments
Experiment 1.1 to 1.4 and 2.1 to 2.2 are conducted solely for the Gulf of Riga as the primary
research area. The performance of the model on the other areas is then evaluated in experi­
ments 1.5 and 2.3. All models are trained on DTU’s gbar server, using a Tesla A100 PCIE 80
GB GPU with a maximal run time of 24 hours. To ensure that the models are deterministic for
the comparison of different setups, seeds are fixed for random number generation.
6.1
Performance Evaluation
The models are evaluated and compared based on visual examinations as well as the root mean
square error (RMSE), the normalized root mean square error (NRMSE) and the coefficient of
determination (R2).
42
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:61==
The RMSE value is a measure to quantify the dissimilarity between the true value and the
predicted value and is calculated for all validation pixels in the dataset. It is calculated like:
RMSE =
v
u
u
t 1
N
N
X
n=1
(xn −yn)2
(6.1)
where N is the number of validation pixels, x is the observed value and y the predicted value.
It varies between 0 and infinite and has the same unit as the observed variable. A RMSE value
close to zero indicates a small error and thus a better model performance.
To compare the different areas to each other, the RMSE is normalized, in order to make the
results comparable. The normalized RMSE (NRMSE) can be considered as a coefficient of
variation, as it shows the extent of variability of the prediction, RMSE, in relation to the mean of
the population x. It is calculated like:
NRMSE = RMSE
x
(6.2)
where x is the mean value of the observed dataset. This normalization is important to com­
pare the different areas, as they have different average values, with the Gulf of Riga showing
substantially larger CHL concentrations than the other two areas.
The R2 value measures the proportion of variance that can be explained by the correlation
between the true value and the predicted value. It is calculated like:
R2 = 1 −
PN
n=1 (yn −xn)2
PN
n=1(yn −y)2
(6.3)
where N is the number of samples, x the observed value, y the predicted value and y the mean
of the predicted dataset. The R2 value therefore quantifies how linear the relationship between
the true value x and the predicted value y is, ranging from 0 for no linear relationship to 1 for a
perfect linear relationship, indicating a better model performance.
6.2
Gap­Filling
The first research question asks if deep learning models can effectively be used to fill gaps in
CHL concentrations. In order to address this question, based on the area of the Gulf of Riga
several experiments are conducted to find the optimal model setups, by analysing the model
response for varying data availability and gap sizes. Before the data can be passed to the
models for training, it must be prepared to fit the model requirements and split into test, training
and validation datasets.
Unless differently stated, all tests are performed with both, the MULTI and the OLCI dataset, the
latter with the initial threshold of data >= 1 in the Gulf of Riga. The DINCAE and DINpCAE mod­
els are trained for a maximum of 500 epochs with early stopping in case that no improvement
can be observed for more than 50 iterations. The initial learning rate of 0.00153 is reduced by a
factor of 0.01 after 30 iterations with no improvement and the batch size is 10. The pCONV3D
model is trained with an initial learning rate of 0.005 for a maximum of 100 epochs with a batch
size of 4. The learning rate is reduced by a factor of 0.01 after 10 iterations with no improvement
of the loss function and is stopped early if no improvement can be observed over 40 epochs.
The final model decision is made based on the model configuration that results in the best
performance for the OLCI dataset.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
43
==PAGE:62==
6.2.1
Training Data Preparation
Depending on the model, the data is prepared in a different way, as the DINCAE and pCONV3D
models have different data requirements. The DINCAE and DINpCAE models add additional
features such as information regarding the day of year and the latitude/longitude. Furthermore,
the DINCAE and DINpCAE training data consists just of one focussed day, with the previous two
days and next two days added as channels to the data. On the other hand the pCONV3D model
requires a multi day block of data at once to perform the 3D convolutions for the gap­filling.
Perlin Noise for Cloud Generation
To train the model to fill gaps in the data, it is necessary to exclude certain data from the original
images to serve as a validation set for evaluating gap predictions. Various methods exist for
creating these validation gaps. One approach involves utilizing real cloud masks of random
days within the training dataset. Another method entails generating artifical clouds. A method
that has shown good results in generating artificial clouds is Perlin noise [151]. Considering
the extensive size of the gaps in the project data, the utilization of real cloud masks from the
training data is considered impractical and alternative validation masks are generated using
Perlin noise.
Perlin noise, originally introduced by K.Perlin [152] in 1985, is a method to generate coherent
noise that generates natural­looking patterns and textures. It involves generating a sequence
of smooth, random values that vary in space. To generate the noise, two main components are
required, a noise function and an interpolation function to approximate the noise values. The
noise function takes an integer input and returns a random number, which remains consistent
for the same input. The interpolation function approximates the noise values based on the
surrounding points. Perlin noise is created by combining multiple layers (octaves) of noise.
Each octave represents a different frequency and amplitude of noise. The noise functions of
different octaves are combined using appropriate weights to create the final Perlin noise.
Algorithm 1: Validation Gaps Generator
Input: t, n
Output: noise
noise ←zeros array of shape (t, n, n);
for i in range(time) do
temp_noise ←generate perlin noise with parameters (n, n), (2, 2), 3, 0.4, 2;
random_noise_dense ←random array of 0’s and 1’s with shape (n, n) and probability
0.5;
random_noise ←random array of 0’s and 1’s with shape (n, n) and probability 0.9 for 0
and 0.1 for 1;
// Apply thresholds to apply random noise based on perlin noise
noise[i, :, :] ←where (|temp_noise| < a × max(|temp_noise|)) set to 0, else set to
random_noise;
noise[i, :, :] ←where (|temp_noise| < b × max(|temp_noise|)) set to noise[i, :, :], else
set to random_noise_dense;
noise[i, :, :] ←where (|temp_noise| < c × max(|temp_noise|)) set to noise[i, :, :], else
set to 1;
end
return noise
While Perlin noise generates continuous values within a defined range, for the validation mask
binary values are required. Therefore, for the cloud generation in the project, Perlin noise is
thresholded to layer different densities of binary noise, resulting in a cloud­like mask. Algorithm
1 shows the pseudo function to demonstrate this approach, using the python package perlin­
44
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:63==
noise [153]. For each day, a random Perlin noise mask is generated. Based on the values in
this mask and the defined thresholds a, b and c, the binary mask value is set to either binary
noise with 10% ones, 50% ones or all ones, resulting in a cloud­like binary mask.
An output of such a mask with values a=0.35, b=0.46 and c=0.58 can be seen in figure 6.2.
The figure shows from left to right the binary mask that is generated with the validation gaps
generator, the land mask that masks out land masses, the training data input with the validation
gaps that are omitted marked in red and initial missing data in white and the reference data for
model training with validation part shaded in red and missing data in white. The parameters a,
b and c are randomly assigned to omit 5 to 95% of the available data during training and to omit
in average 20% of the data for validation.
Figure 6.2: Example of perlin mask with a=0.35, b=0.46 and c=0.58 for validation in Gulf of Riga. Parts of the data
that are omitted for validation are colored in red.
Data Splitting and Validation
In order to ensure a fair comparison between the models and to judge the model performance
on unseen data, the validation method of ’leave time out’ is implemented. To do so, the data
is divided into 20­day blocks, which are then split into training, testing and validation datasets.
The models are trained only on the training dataset, with the validation dataset to analyse the
during­training performance of the models on unseen data. The models are then assessed
solely on the test datasets that the models have not seen at any time before.
The ratio between training data, testing data and validation data is chosen to be 80% of the data
for training and 10% for testing and validation in the time frame of 04.09.1997 to 31.12.2021 for
the MULTI and 30.04.2016 to 14.04.2023 for the OLCI dataset. In order for the model to learn
how to fill gaps correctly, artificial gaps are randomly generated that are supposed to imitate
clouds as described in Section 6.2.1. The input data is masked with those gaps, omitting data
for training and validation purposes. During the model training, the generated values in the
validation gaps are compared to the true values in these gaps and the models are solely trained
on the gapped data. When evaluating the model performance, the gaps are used to assess the
performance of the models. Therefore, to ensure comparability across the models, the same
gaps are generated for all tests.
DINCAE and DINpCAE
For the DINpCAE model and DINCAE model, the data preprocessing follows the procedure of
Barth et al. [86]. Additionally to the CHL concentration, further features that can be directly
derived from the CHL dataset are added as channels to the input data. The first channel is the
variance of the transformed data, data −mean, with data being the CHL value for the 20­day
time frame and mean being the temporal mean of each such dataset. The second channel is
the initial uncertainty, which is defined to be 1 −mask(data), so that known pixels have zero
uncertainty and unknown pixels have an uncertainty of 1. Furthermore, the cloud mask of the
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
45
==PAGE:64==
data is added in an extra channel. In order to help the model capture the annual variations
of CHL blooms in the Baltic Sea, the sine and cosine of the day of year are included as extra
channels. Also the normalized longitude and latitude is added, in order to help the model learn
spatial patterns that are associated with specific locations of the inspected area. The last eight
channels are filled with the data and cloud masks of the previous and following 2 days in the
dataset. Given the large variation between the two datasets MULTI and OLCI, additionally a
binary channel is added to the data, indicating with zeros that the data is MULTI and ones that
the data is part of the OLCI dataset. Thus the final input data includes a total of 15 channels
that are passed to model. The final input for the DINCAE model is:
X(160, 160, 15), Y (160, 160, 2)
With X(160, 160, 15) being the input to the model with the 15 input channels and Y (160, 160, 2)
the target output with the two channels representing the mean and uncertainty.
For the DINpCAE model, an additional mask tensor is generated, that consists of a binary mask
that indicates where data is available and where not. The mask (M) is duplicated to have the
same shape as the input data (X) and additionally passed to the model. The final input data to
the model, together with the expected output is thus:
[X(160, 160, 15), M(160, 160, 15)], Y (160, 160, 2)
With [X(160, 160, 15), M(160, 160, 15)] being the input to the model, including the mask with the
according shapes and Y (160, 160, 2) the target output with the two channels representing the
mean and uncertainty.
pCONV3D
The pCONV3D model takes the whole 20­day block of multiple satellite images as well as a
corresponding binary mask, indicating where data is available and where not, as input. To
prepare the data for training, only the binary information about the datasets is added as an
extra channel to the data.
The final input data to the model, together with the expected output is thus:
[X(160, 160, 20, 1), M(160, 160, 20, 1)], Y (160, 160, 20)
With [X(160, 160, 20, 1), M(160, 160, 20, 1)] being the input to the model with the one channel
indicating the dataset and Y (160, 160, 20) the target output, filling gaps for 20 days at once.
6.2.2
Hyperparameter Tuning
Each model has multiple hyperparameters that can be adjusted and have a large influence on
the model performance. Before conducting the experiments, the python package kerastuner
by O’Malley et al. [154] is used to perform preliminary hyperparameter tuning, using Bayesian
optimization as the optimization method to find the optimal combination. The search range
and choice for the different models is shown in table 6.1. For all other hyperparameters the
values that are initially suggested by the authors of the initial models or keras default values
are applied. The DINCAE and DINpCAE model are trained on the same hyperparameters, in
order to see the effect of the model modifications. The hyperparameters are optimized for the
DINCAE model and then applied to the DINpCAE model. For the pCONV3D model, the model
is only optimized for the learning rate.

6.2.3
Experiment 1.1: Pixel Availability
Given the sparsity of the data, a lot of pixels in the satellite images are blank and do not provide
any information for the model training. Omitting these images causes a trade­off between pixel­
availability and total data availability, as only few observations provide high pixel availability, as
shown in figure 6.3. The figure shows that the more pixels are available, the less data samples
are available. The same percentage of samples were defined to be test and validation samples,
therefore in the graph they overlap each other for each dataset. Given that the MULTI dataset
spans a larger time range than the OLCI dataset, overall more MULTI data is available.
Figure 6.3: Pixel availability in training data for Gulf of Riga
In a first experiment the sensitivity of the models to the percentage of available pixels in the
training data is analyzed, taking the Gulf of Riga as the test area. To do so, the models are
trained with varying pixel availability ranging from 10­100% to 50­100%. The pixel availability
is defined as the ratio of available number of pixels for each 20­day block divided by the total
number of pixels in the land­sea mask that are defined to be sea pixels. The data is log trans­
formed and multiplied by a factor of 10 for the DINCAE and DINpCAE model and simply log
transformed for the pCONV3D model. Multiplying the data by a factor of 10 in the DINCAE and
DINpCAE model helps to maintain a similar ratio between the target variable and the additional
features as in the initial application, where the target variable is the sea surface temperature.
Tables 6.2a, 6.2b and 6.2c show the results for all three models, using the RMSE value in mg/m3
together with the 95% confidence intervals and the R2 value for the initial data distribution for
evaluation. It can be observed that while the DINpCAE and DINCAE model show a superior
performance for the MULTI dataset, based on both, the RMSE and R2 value, this result is not as
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
47
==PAGE:66==
obvious for the pCONV3D model. Here, the R2 value for the MULTI dataset also shows better
results, yet the RMSE value is mainly better for the OLCI dataset. For the evaluation of the
model, more emphasis is put on the result of the OLCI dataset, as this is the dataset that will
be used to make the predictions, and thus filling the gaps for this dataset is more crucial for this
study. To define the best model, apart from the RMSE and R2 value, also the data availability is
taken into account. While a large amount of data availability might lead to better results in the
evaluation matrix, the number of training samples is also very small, which can cause limited
generalization of the model as well as a higher sensitivity to noise. Hence, due to increased
training samples and low R2 values, particularly for the OLCI dataset, the decision is made to
use 30­100% pixel availability instead of 40­50%, even though the pCONV3D model trained on
40­100% pixel availability demonstrates lower RMSE values. Similarly, the model trained on
30­100% pixel availability is considered the best for the DINCAE model.

6.2.4
Experiment 1.2: Data Transformation
While deep learning models are generally able to extract features from different data distribu­
tions, data transformation can support the model and thus increase model perfomance. There­
fore, based on the best performing training data availability for the models, the models’ re­
sponse to different data transformations is analysed. Namely, the previous log transformation
is compared to boxcox transformation and min­max­normalization of the data. For the boxcox
transformation, the appropriate lambdas for the entire dataset, split into MULTI and OLCI is pri­
orly defined and used as the parameter for all transformations. Lambda is the parameter that
optimizes the difference between the following data transformation and a normal distribution
curve.
xboxcoxi(λ) =



(xλ
i −1)
λ
,
if λ ̸= 0
log(xi),
if λ = 0
(6.4)
The idea is, that optimizing both datasets individually to fit a normal distribution by using indi­
vidual lambdas for the two datasets, will result in more similar datasets for the training process.
Similarly, the min­max­normalization is also based on the minimum and maximum values of the
entire dataset, split into MULTI and OLCI, and defined by the following formula.
xnormi =
xi −min(Xi)
max(Xi) −min(Xi)
(6.5)
In both cases x represents every value in the entire dataset X and i is the differentiation between
MULTI and OLCI.
Table 6.3 shows the results of the experiment with the RMSE value in mg/m3 together with the
95% confidence interval between 20­day blocks and the R2 value for the initial data distribution,
highlighting the best performing data transformation for the OLCI dataset that will be used for
subsequent experiments. Contrary to the expectation, the DINpCAE and pCONV3D models
achieve the highest performance overall when trained on log­transformed data, while this result
varies for the DINCAE model depending on the used dataset. The DINCAE model performs
better for the OLCI dataset when min­max transformed, yet for the MULTI dataset log trans­
formation is superior. Notably, the pCONV3D model overestimates the values for the MULTI
dataset when transformed with the boxcox transformation.

on performance metrics of OLCI data.

is most optimal at a value of ­0.28 while for the OLCI dataset it is ­0.08. This indicates a larger
skewness in the MULTI dataset. With a more negative lambda, the data transformation is more
influenced by values that lie further away from the mean, which can cause outliers to have a
stronger impact on the transformed data. Meanwhile, a lambda of 0.08 corresponds almost to
a log transformation, as shown in equation 6.2.4, which has shown to be the best performing
transformation method for the pCONV3D model. Also visually, the log transformation shows
the best performance, which confirms the evaluated RMSE and R2 values.
6.2.5
Experiment 1.3: Data Sets
The previous experimental results show a notable disparity between the MULTI and OLCI
datasets, particularly for the DINpCAE and DINCAE models. A potential explanation for this
discrepancy is the unbalanced nature of the training datasets, with a greater amount of data
available for the MULTI dataset compared to the OLCI dataset. This data imbalance can ad­
versely impact the performance of the less represented OLCI dataset, leading to inferior results.
To investigate the influence of the training datasets on the OLCI dataset’s performance, the fol­
lowing experiment compares the performance of models trained on both datasets with that of
models trained solely on the OLCI dataset.
The results as shown in Table 6.4 with the RMSE value in mg/m3 together with the 95% con­
fidence interval and the R2 value for the initial data distribution show that all models when
trained only on the OLCI dataset perform worse on the OLCI dataset, than when trained on
both datasets.
Interestingly, the pCONV3D model achieves very low RMSE values for the
MULTI dataset, when trained solely in the OLCI dataset. A likely cause for this behaviour is
the increased variability in the OLCI data, so when trained on this more challenging dataset
the model performs better when predicting the less noisy MULTI dataset. However, the perfor­
mance of the OLCI dataset is not increased by just training the model on OLCI data and the
experiments will thus continue with the combined datasets.

in strong green, based on performance metrics of OLCI data.

6.2.6
Experiment 1.4: Gap Sizes
Based on the best performing models of previous experiments, the models’ performance for
varying gap sizes is analysed. To do so, the four test datasets from the MULTI and the OLCI
dataset with the highest pixel availability are selected and modified to have 45, 55, 65, 75,
85 and 95% of the data missing by adjusting the values of a, b and c in the validation gaps
generator. This corresponds to 5, 20, 25, 55, 75 and 90% of the available data, as even the
datasets with the highest pixel availability only have 55 to 65% of the pixels available. In order to
cover the worst­case scenario, the missing data corresponds to the entire 20­day time blocks,
instead of single images within these time blocks. In the real data the number of available pixels
varies on a day­to­day basis and it is unlikely to have 20 days with consistently less than 5%
data available.

value in mg/m3 together with 95% confidence interval between datasets, and R2 value calculated for initial data
distribution. Final model choice based on performance metrics highlighted in strong green.

Table 6.5 shows the results of the experiment, using the RMSE value in mg/m3, along with
the 95% interval, and R2 values for the initial data distribution for evaluation. First of all, it
is noticeable that all RMSE values are larger than in the previous experiments. The reason
for that is most likely the choice of datasets for this experiment. The data with more than
50% pixel availability corresponds to the time periods 15.04.2005 ­ 05.05.2005, 08.05.2012 ­
27.05.2012, 02.06.2015 ­ 21.06.2015 and 16.06.2016 ­ 05.07.2016 for the MULTI dataset, and
the time periods 10.07.2018 ­ 29.07.2018, 06.05.2019 ­ 25.05.2019, 24.06.2021 ­ 13.07.2021
and 29.07.2022 ­ 17.08.2022 for the OLCI dataset. These dates all fall within the expected
season of spring and summer blooms, as explained in Section 2.1, which can be considered
’outlier seasons’. Therefore, the plankton dynamics are harder to predict in these time periods
and thus most likely causing the increased overall RMSE values in this experiment.
When focusing on the actual results of the experiment, it can be seen that, interestingly, the
RMSE values for the pCONV3D model improve when more data is omitted from the model,
while the R2 value successively decreases. Meanwhile, the DINCAE and DINpCAE models
demonstrate unexpectedly consistent performance even when faced with significant data gaps.
To investigate these surprising outcomes further, a visual analysis of the model performance
with varying gap sizes is conducted.
Figure 6.6 shows a comparison of the gap­filling capacities of the models for the tested per­
centages of missing data. The figure reveals that the pCONV3D model is not able to capture
the dynamics of the data when faced with 75% or more data missing, and heavily underesti­
mates the values after. When provided with more data, the model tends to overestimate the
values, which likely causes the larger RMSE values. Figure 6.6 further shows, that the DINCAE
and DINpCAE models consistently predict almost the same outcome, disregarding the provided
data segments. Further visualizations with varying gap sizes are attached in Appendix A.3.1.
A likely cause for this behavior can be attributed to the post­ and pre­processing procedure
employed. Following the processing procedure described in the first model by Barth et al. [86],
the mean of each dataset is subtracted from the data before being fed into the model. When
52
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:71==
Figure 6.6: Comparison of gap­filling performance of the three different models with varying gap sizes for the
18.03.2005. Missing percentages refer to entire 20­day time block.
reconstructing the data after making predictions, the same mean is added back to the predic­
tions. However, considering the dynamic nature of the plankton population, the mean is not
constant for every 20­day dataset. Consequently, the model struggles to grasp the variability
in the data accurately. When omitting a substantial 95% of the data, the model simply predicts
the temporal mean of the 20­day period. This experiment reveals that the model itself is un­
able to enhance the performance beyond this temporal mean estimation. Unfortunately, due
to time constraints, further investigation of potential solutions are not conducted in this project.
Consequently, the data preparation for the subsequent predictions solely involve the pCONV3D
model, which will be used to fill the data gaps in order to train the predictions on gap­filled data.
6.2.7
Experiment 1.5: Areas
To analyse the suitability of the model to fill gaps representative for the whole Baltic Sea, the
best performing pCONV3D model is individually trained with data from all three areas. The
training lasts for a maximum number of 120 epochs, with a maximum runtime of 24 hours.
Table 6.6 demonstrates the performance of the model in each area, evaluated by the NRMSE,
R2 value and RMSE value in mg/m3, accompanied by the 95% confidence intervals to represent
the level of statistical uncertainty. The model shows a superior performance for the Gulf of Riga
data, as indicated by the lowest NRMSE and the highest R2 value for the OLCI dataset. This
outcome is consistent with expectations, considering that the model was specifically optimized
for the Gulf of Riga area. Notably, across all areas the NSRME values consistently favor the
OLCI dataset over the MULTI dataset.
This suggests that the model manages to perform more accurate predictions on the OLCI
dataset. However, in the Southern Kattegat and Gotland Basin area the uncertainty of pre­
dictions is also larger for the OLCI dataset, as evidenced by the larger confidence intervals. On
the other hand, the R2 value is consistently better for the MULTI dataset, indicating a better fit
to the overall trend. Especially in the Gotland Basin a large discrepancy between the R2 values
of the two datasets can be observed. While in that region, the R2 value is better for the MULTI
dataset than in the other areas, it also has the worst R2 value for the OLCI dataset.

Figure 6.8 shows the full distribution and residuals, for the Gulf of Riga (a), Southern Kattegat
(b) and Gotland Basin (c). The distibutions are again presented once in the log transformed
and once in the original data distribution. One can clearly see, that the Southern Kattegat
and Gotland Basin have large individual outliers, that the model is unable to capture. Overall,
a downward slope of the residuals can be observed, indicating that the model is not able to
reconstruct extreme values in the dataset and focusses around the mean, not capturing the full
width of the original distribution. However, overall the model is able to roughly reconstruct the
initial distribution of the validation pixels for all areas.
In order to get a better idea of the actual model performance in the different areas, a visual
analysis is conducted for one randomly picked sample from the test dataset of all three areas.
Figure 6.9 shows the image time series for three consecutive days, together with the model input
data, the prediction and the prediction error. Figure 6.10 shows the corresponding distribution
of the true and the predicted values of the validation pixels as well as the residuals. The images
are labelled (A),(B),(C) and (a), (b), (c) for the MULTI and OLCI datset respectively of the Gulf of
Riga, (D), (E), (F) and (d), (e), (f) for the MULTI and OLCI dataset respectively of the Southern
Kattegat and (G), (H), (I) and (d), (h), (i) for the MULTI and OLCI dataset of the Southern Gotland
area, always showing three consecutive days.
It can be seen for the Gulf of Riga that the model has trouble to reconstruct all dynamics that are
present in the initial image, both for the MULTI and OLCI dataset. The time series and residual
plots show that the model underestimates the initial values. All residual plots show a downward
slope, starting above zero and ending far below, indicating that the model is unable to capture
extreme values and especially underestimates large values. The histograms of the true and
predicted distributions shows the same, as the distribution of the predicted values is focused
around the middle, not capturing the full width of the original distribution.
For the Kattegat region, the model is able to capture the dynamics better, which might also be
attributed to the fact that the plankton distribution is very static in the sample testset of both, the
MULTI and OLCI data. Also the residuals show that especially for the MULTI dataset the model
is well able to capture the original distribution. For the OLCI dataset, again a downward slope
of the residuals can be observed, indicating an underestimation of larger values.

struggles for the Gotland Basin. The Kattegat area performs best from the visual inspection,
but outliers reduce the overall model performance, as the model is not able to capture those
adequately. In the Gotland Basin a very strong smoothing effect can be observed that hinders
the model to reconstruct individual dynamics.

To pass the data on to the prediction part of the project, the original data is kept where available

was used for the previous visualization, keeping the original data where possible. Looking at
the whole 20­day time block provides a good impression of the models’ gap­filling abilities.
Eventhough confronted with partly substantial gaps and even entirely missing days, the model
is able to fill the gaps with satisfactory results. Especially for the Southern Kattegat it is hard to
distinguish between gap­filled and original data. In the Gulf of Riga and especially the Gotland
Basin however, the gap­filled part of each image can be more easily distinguished because of
the before­mentioned smoothed texture that is present in the gaps that the model has filled,
leading to a relatively noisy texture where gap­filled and original data is present. The model
only manages to reconstruct individual dynamics and swirls when the gaps are small and the
gapped image is surrounded by mainly available images, as for example in the second last day
(lower row, second image from the right) in the Gotland Basin.
Additionally, the visualization also shows the overall high level of variability in the initial data,
especially in the Gotland Basin the level of CHL concentration varies largely on a daily basis,
such as for example between the 16th and 17th day (lower row, first and second image from left),
where the concentration on the 16th day is evidently larger than on the 17th day, questioning
once again the reliability of the data. Based on these observations, the model shows a relatively
consistent performance for filling the gaps of the available data.

6.2.8
Comparison to Interpolation Methods
To evaluate the usability of the proposed deep learning model to fill the gaps in the CHL con­
centration dataset in the Baltic Sea, the best model output for each area is compared to four
common interpolation techniques:
1. Total mean of each 20­day time block
2. Temporal mean per pixel of each 20­day time block
3. K­nearest­neighbor interpolation method (KNN)
4. Ordinary kriging (OK)
For the KNN interpolation method, KKNImputer by sklearn [155], with the number of nearest
neighbors set to 5 and weighted distances is applied. The OK approach uses the OrdinaryK­
riging method by pykrige [156] with a linear variogram model, implementing 2D kriging on a
daily resolution. A small description of the latter two methods is provided in Appendix C. The
KNN and OK method are run in just two dimensions, as the run time and use of computational
resources increases drastically when applied to 3D data.
Table 6.7 shows the results based on the RMSE value with 95% confidence interval and R2
value for the initial distribution for filling the validation gaps in the test dataset. Based on the
highlighted best­performing values in each row, it is easy to see that the previous observation
hold true also when comparing the model performance with interpolation methods. While the
model performance in the Gulf of Riga area is primarily better than all interpolation models, the
performance decreases for the Southern Kattegat and especially the Gotland Basin. In the Got­
land Basin the OK method fully outperforms the pCONV3D model. Given the low performance
that could be observed for the Gotland Basin already from the previous experiment this result
is not very surprising. For the Kattegat region, using the temporal mean results in consider­
ably good results, given the simplicity of the method, indicating again a rather static plankton
distribution in the area.

Eventhough the OK2D method shows superior results in the Gotland Basin and partly for the
Southern Kattegat, it must be pointed out that the method has two significant disadvantages, as
Appel [82] already points out in his study. Firstly, the runtime and computational power that is
needed to perform the interpolation are severely larger than that of the pCONV3D model, once
the model is trained. Interpolating the gaps in one single 20­day time block took in average 5403
seconds when using the OK method. In comparison, for the trained pCONV3D it was less than
0.6 seconds. Even when considering the training time of approximately 8 hours when training
the model for 100 epochs, the pConv3D model is still faster when interpolating the whole test
dataset. The second disadvantage of the OK method when applied in two dimensions is its
disability to fill temporal gaps in the data. As soon as there is all data missing for one day, the
OK method can not be applied. Given the severe data sparsity of the Baltic Sea region, this is
a large draw back from applying 2D methods on the dataset. In contrast, the pCONV3D model
performs well also with data with varying gap size, meaning data where the amount of missing
data varies on a day to day basis. Due to the convolutions, already a few days with a higher
data availability are sufficient for the model to reconstruct the distribution, as shown in Figure
6.11.
Overall, the comparison shows that the presented deep learning model is compatible also with
more sophisticated interpolation methods. Especially when optimized for the area, as is the
case for the Gulf of Riga, the model can be applied as an efficient, fast method to fill gaps in the
data.
6.3
Prediction
Research question two challenges the numerical model by asking if deep learning models might
outperform those. In order to address this question, a series of experiments are conducted to
determine the optimal model configuration for predicting the CHL concentration. These experi­
ments build upon the gap­filling process by utilizing the gap­filled data as the input for the pre­
diction models. The main research area is once again the Gulf of Riga, that is used to analyse
the model sensitivity to different data transformations and to optimize the hyperparameters to
increase the model performance. The datasets used in this experiment include the CHL dataset
along with 14 other features that are used as input variables to the models. Due to limited his­
torical data availability across all datasets, the experiments use solely the more recent OLCI
dataset for the CHL concentrations, excluding the MULTI dataset from the prediction process.
Unless explicitly stated otherwise, the models are trained for a maximum of 100 epochs im­
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
61
==PAGE:80==
plementing early stopping if no improvement in the loss function is observed for more than 20
epochs. Furthermore, the learning rate is reduced after 10 epochs of no improvement.
6.3.1
Training Data Preparation
To prepare the data for the predictions, all gaps in the OLCI dataset are filled, using the best
performing pCONV3D model and leaving the original data where possible. A visual example of
such a filled dataset is shown in Figure 6.11. The additional features are then added as extra
channels to the data, resulting in a total of 15 channels with the following features: (1) CHL
concentration, (2) Cloud mask, (3) Wind v10, (4) Wind u10, (5) Precipitation, (6) Air pressure,
(7) Radiation, (8) SST, (9) Wave VHM0, (10) Wave VMDR, (11) Topography, (12) Cosine of day
of year, (13) Sine of day of year, (14) Latitude, (15) Longitude.
All parameters except for the CHL concentration are normalized using min­max normalization
to range between 0 and 1, while the optimal data transformation for the CHL concentration is
tested in the first experiment.
Data Splitting and Validation
While a common approach in time series modelling is to split the data based on a certain point
in time and use the data before for training and after for evaluation, due to the data scarcity
and the ambivalent results of the gap­filling procedure, a different approach is taken in this
study. Therefore, similar to the approach taken in the gap­filling training, the evaluation of
the model performance on unseen data employs the ”leave time out” (LTO) cross­validation
method using 20­day blocks. To ensure higher data quality, any data with initially less than 30%
pixel availability is omitted from the experiments. The remaining data is then split into training,
testing and validation datasets, each consisting of 20­day blocks. During the training the data is
trained on the training dataset and validated on the validation dataset. The model performance
is assessed solely on the testing dataset. 80% of the data is again used for training and 10% for
testing and validating the models, spanning a time frame from 30.04.2016 to 14.04.2023. Since
only the OLCI dataset is utilized in these experiments, the available data is smaller compared
to the gap­filling phase. Therefore, to increase the amount of data for training, a sliding window
scheme commonly used in LSTM models [157] is employed within the 20­day windows. This
scheme involves training the model to predict the next 5 days based on the previous 10 days
of data. Consequently, within each 20­day window, six seperate data frames can be extracted,
as illustrated in Figure 6.12.
Figure 6.12: Rolling window training scheme for ConvLSTM model, based on 20 day blocks
For each 20­day time series with sequential observations X = [x0, x1, x2, ..., x20], the rolling
window scheme creates input­output pairs based on a window that includes a historical and a
target part. The window is slid over the time series and at each position the historical part of
62
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:81==
the window is extracted as the input sequence and the target part as the output target. Each
input sequence at time t is thus Xt = [xt, xt+1, xt+2, ..., xt+h] and each output target at time t is
yt = [yt+h+1, yt+h+2, ..., yt+h+o] with h as the size of the historical part of the window and o as
the size of the output target part of the window. Based on the small number of available 20­
day data blocks, in order to get the maximum number of training while still predicting sufficient
days ahead to compare the outcome to the numerical model predictions, it is defined to predict
always 5 days ahead, based on 10 previous days. Thus, the historical part is defined to be 10
and the output target part is defined to be 5. Using this method a total of 222 training samples
and 30 testing and validation samples are generated for the Gulf of Riga, that will be used for
the first model experiments.
Final Input Size
As previously mentioned, 10 days are used as input to the model and 5 days are the expected
output target. For the input data, each day must include all 15 features in additional channels.
The output target only includes the CHL concentration for the 5 target days, as this is the only
feature that we want to predict. The size of the final input data to the model, together with the
expected output is thus:
X(10, 160, 160, 15), Y (1, 160, 160, 5)
X(10, 160, 160, 15) is the input of the model, having the historical days in the first channel, the
dimensions of each image in the second and third channel and the features in the last channel.
Respectively, Y (1, 160, 160, 5) is the output of the model, having the features in the first channel,
the dimension in the second and third and the target days in the last channel. The first and last
channel are reversed in the input and output data, due to the internal structure of the different
utilized input and output layers in keras, as a ConvLSTM layer is used as input and a Conv3D
layer is used as output. These layers have different setups that have to be accommodated in
order to train the model effectively.
6.3.2
Hyperparameter Tuning
In contrast to the hyperparameter tuning strategy employed for the gap­filling models, a differ­
ent approach is adopted for the prediction models. Instead of optimizing the hyperparameters
upfront and conducting experiments based on the optimized model, the hyperparameters are
initially set to be the same for both models. This choice is made to analyze the sensitivity of
the models to varying data transformations. By keeping the hyperparameters consistent across
both models, the impact of different data transformations on the model performance can be
compared. This analysis helps to determine the optimal data transformation approach for the
task at hand. Once the optimal data transformation is identified, the hyperparameters are sub­
sequently optimized in Experiment 2.2 using Bayesian optimization. This two­step approach
enables a fair comparison between the models while still allowing for individual optimization to
identify the most suitable model for the specific task.
The following 8 hyperparameters will be set and adjusted during the experiments: (1) learning
rate, (2) batch size, (3) filter sizes, (4) kernel size of 1. layer, (5) kernel size of 2. layer, (6)
kernel size of 3. layer, (7) activation function, (8) kernel size of final 3D convolution. For all other
hyperparameters the default values used Lin et al. [111] when constructing the SaConvLSTM
model or keras default values are used.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
63
==PAGE:82==
6.3.3
Experiment 2.1: Data Transformation
The first experiment analyses the sensitivity of the models to different data transformations of the
CHL data. In this experiment the hyperparameters are set to be the following for both models:
(1) learning rate = 0.001, (2) batch size = 2, (3) filter sizes = (16,32,64), (4) kernel size of 1.
layer = 3, (5) kernel size of 2. layer = 3, (6) kernel size of 3. layer = 3, (7) activation function =
tanh, (8) kernel size of final 3D convolution = 3.
The tested transformations differ slightly from those tested in the gap­filling process. Due to the
uncertain results of the box­cox transformation in the previous gap­filling process, this trans­
formation is not applied again in the prediction part. Instead, an alternative transformation ap­
proach combining the log transformation and min­max normalization is tested. This approach
involves applying the log transformation first and then subsequently applying min­max normal­
ization. Additionally the familiar log transformation and the min­max normalization, that were
also tested for the gap­filling models and as explained in Section 6.2.4 are applied.
Table 6.8 shows that the log transformation performs strongly for short term predictions but the
performance decreases for multi­step predictions. For the ConvLSTM model, the log trans­
formation is also superior in total, when considering the RMSE value. When considering the
R2 value, the min­max transformation is slightly better, yet the advantage is only very low and
therefore the log transformation is considered the best choice for the ConvLSTM model.

For the Sa­ConvLSTM model, the min­max normalization performs best overall for both the
RMSE and R2 value. It is remarkable that the transformation that first employs a log transfor­
mation and then applies min­max normalization on the log transformed data performs noticeably
worse for both models. However, a possible explanation is that the combination of transforma­
tions may lead to a loss of valuable information by distorting the original data distribution. In
relation to the other features the CHL distribution gains a different magnitude by applying both
the log transformation and the min­max normalization. This shift might hinder the model from
extracting relevant features to make reliable predictions.
6.3.4
Experiment 2.2: Parameter Optimization
Based on the result of the previous experiment, the hyperparameters of the models are op­
timized, again using the python package kerastuner by O’Malley et al. [154] with Bayesian
optimisation. For this experiment, the optimization is run three times for each model. First,
all 8 previously mentioned hyperparameters are optimized at once, running each trial for 100
epochs for a duration of 24 hours. Hyperparameter (2) to (8) are then set based on the low­
est validation loss that is achieved during the optimization and the learning rate is fine tuned,
once applying log sampling and once applying normal sampling for another 24 hours each. The
two sampling methods differ in the way, that while the normal samppling method samples the
64
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:83==
learning rate uniformly within the given bounds, log sampling samples logarithmical, so that the
difference between two consecutive parameters increases multiplicatively, allowing to cover a
broader range of values while focusing on the more promising search space. Employing the
log sampling method for the learning rate yields the lowest validation loss for both models. The
convergence behaviour of the optimization process for both the initial as well as the refined
optimizations is visualized in Figure 6.13. The convergence patterns reveal that the optimiza­
tions have a non­smooth behaviour, particularly during the initial optimization, where the model
shows substantial fluctuations. This can be attributed to the extensive search space that
(a) Optimization convergence ConvLSTM
(b) Optimization convergence Sa­ConvLSTM
(c) Validation loss/learning rate ConvLSTM
(d) Validation loss/learning rate Sa­ConvLSTM
Figure 6.13: Optimization convergences and validation loss development over changing learning rates for ConvL­
STM and Sa­ConvLSTM model. Dashed lines in (a) and (b) indicate the fitted linear regression for each optimization
method, indicating overall convergence behaviour.
the initial optimization explores, considering the simultaneous optimization of 8 hyperparam­
eters. In contrast, the refinement optimizations demonstrate a more continuous convergence
pattern, eventhough the Sa­ConvLSTM model still shows significant jumps. Nevertheless, fit­
ting a linear regression curve to the loss output reveals a negative slope, indicating a decreasing
trend in validation loss throughout the refinement optimization process. In the initial optimization
process the slope of the fitted linear regression is positive, yet when looking at the correspond­
ing figure it becomes apparent that this trend can be attributed to the presence of larger outliers
in later iterations. Figures 6.13c and 6.13d further show zoom­ins of the validation loss for differ­
ent learning rates, indicating the optimal value that was found in the optimization process. For
the ConvLSTM model a learning rate of 0.0002579 yields the lowest validation loss, while for
the Sa­ConvLSTM model it is a learning rate of 9.2709e­05. A more thorough visual analysis of
the learning rate convergance during the optimization process is appended in Appendix A.4.1.
Based on the tuning outcome, the optimal set of hyperparameters is selected as presented in
Table 6.9.

Table 6.10 shows the model result after the optimization compared to the result before the op­
timization process, which is the best performing setup of the previous experiment. Against the
expectations, the optimization has not resulted in better performance for both models. While
the performance of the ConvLSTM model was improved by the optimization process, the per­
formance of the Sa­ConvLSTM model dropped. A possible explanation for this behaviour is the
extended depth of the Sa­ConvLSTM model in combination with the small validation dataset
that the model was optimized on. Due to the increased number of nodes and therefore weights
in the model, it is better able to capture small changes and features in the dataset. However,
the dataset in the experiment is small, with only 37 initial training datasets and 5 initial validation
datasets, resulting in 222 training and 30 validation sets after implementation of the rolling win­
dow scheme. Most likely, the Sa­ConvLSTM model has identified noise in the validation dataset
as important features and optimized the model to fit this noise. While improving the performance
on the validation dataset by doing so, the overall performance on other datasets such as the
66
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:85==
test dataset decreases drastically, as can be observed in the experiment. Based on this ob­
servation, it can be assumed that the complexity of the Sa­ConvLSTM model is not suitable for
the provided setup. Furthermore, the ConvLSTM model outperforms the initial Sa­ConvLSTM
model, when considering the RMSE value. Therefore, the ConvLSTM model is chosen as the
final model to perform the predictions.
6.3.5
Experiment 2.3: Areas
To analyse the performance of the optimized ConvLSTM model to predict the CHL concentration
in other parts of the Baltic Sea, the model is trained individually on the data from all three
predefined subareas. The training lasts for a total of 100 epochs with a maximum runtime of 24
hours. The NRMSE value is used as an additional validation metrics together with the RMSE
and R2 values in order to make the outcome comparable between the three areas.
Table 6.11 shows the comparison between the three areas, evaluated by the RMSE value and
NRMSE value with 95% confident interval and R2 value, calculated for the initial data distribu­
tion. As previously in the gap­filling model, the prediction performs best for the Gulf of Riga,
based on the NRMSE value. However, this time the prediction model generalizes better for
the Gotland Basin than for the Southern Kattegat, showing better performance in that area,
based on the NRMSE value, also when taking into account the larger uncertainty in this area.
When looking at the R2 value of all areas, the model performs best for the Southern Kattegat,

the Gulf of Riga, which can also be attributed to the overall smaller, respectively larger values
for the specific area.

6.3.6
Comparison to Baseline Methods
A common technique to verify the performance of deep learning models is to compare the model
to baseline values that are easy replicable and reliable. In this study the model is compared to
the following two baseline methods:
1. Naive method: The naive method uses the last known day’s value as the prediction for
the next five days. This method assumes that the future values will remain a constant or
have a a similar pattern as the most recent observation.
2. Historical average method: The historical average method uses the mean of the known,
historical 10 days as the prediction for the next five days. Thereby the assumption is made
that the future values will be centered around the historical average of each time window.

6.3.7
Comparison to Numerical Model and In­Situ Measurements
Note: Initially the month of May was chosen for the comparison between deep learning pre­
dictions and numerical forecasts and the numerical forecast data was continuously downladed
between the 06.05.2023 to 30.05.2023 during this month. However, due to a technical error
in the Copernicus Marine Service, no OLCI data was available for the month of May to make
the deep learning prediction. This was discovered at the 20th of June, after contacting the
service, as a delay of some days was expected for the service to upload the data. In order
to still compare some deep learning predictions to numerical data forecasts as well as in­situ
measurement, the forecasts were downloaded daily for 5 days ahead between the 20th and
24th of June, generating data for the time span of 20th to 28th of June. Respectively the in­situ
measurements were downloaded for the time span of 20th of June to 28th of June and used for
the comparison.
In order to assess the quality of the model predictions, these are compared to the forecasts of
the numerical model as well as in­situ data. For simplicity, in the following section the numerical
forecast data will be addressed with ” num. forecast” and the deep learning predictions using
the ConvLSTM model will be called ”DL predictions”.
Before the different data sources can be compared, they must be transformed to the same
spatial resolution. For the num. forecasts this is done using the interp_like function from the
xarray library [146], interpolating the num. forecast data to the same spatial resolution as the
DL predictions.
For the in­situ data a different approach must be taken, as the data is not present as spatial
data but rather as punctual data, identified by the latitude and longitude of the exact position
where the sample was taken. To compare the data with the grid­like num. forecasts and DL
predictions, the individual measurements are binned into the same grid resolution as the DL
predictions. If several measurements fall into the same grid cell, the mean is taken as the
respective value. This way it can be ensured that the same position in the num. forecasts, the
DL predictions and the in­situ data are getting compared.
Due to the before mentioned external technical problem, the availability of in­situ data is small
and only data for the Gotland Basin is available with irregular sampling. This results in avail­
able in­situ data for the following four days: 20.06, 21.06., 23.06. and 27.06., with 444, 415,
398 and 389 measurements, binned into 61, 61, 59 and 51 grid cells respectively. In order to
make the most of this sparse dataset, the rolling window scheme is applied on the predictions,
always predicting the next five days and then moving one day ahead, before again predicting
the next five days. The gaps of the data is filled, based on previous 20 days, as it is assumed
that the future values are not known. The results for each day that is forecasted ahead are then
compared to the numerical forecasts of the same day ahead and the in­situ data and accumu­
lated, as previously in the experiment part of the predictions. For the Gulf of Riga and Southern
Kattegat, the predictions of the num. forecasts and DL predictions are compared, without any
in­situ data for validation.

6.4
Feature Analysis
A big drawback of deep learning models is their black box behaviour, that makes it hard to
understand what is going on inside the model and to draw conclusions about the relationships
between the input features and the output values. Previous studies have mainly included fea­
tures based on domain knowledge without determining, which features are actually relevant for
the model output [158]. To confront this problem, the goal of the presented feature analysis is
to understand which features are most important for the utilized deep learning model to make
predictions.
To analyse the feature importance in this study and thus address the third research question,
a combination of approaches are followed. First, a method called feature permutation analysis
is employed [159]. Thereby, the decrease in the model performance for each predicted day
is evaluated when single feature values are randomly shuffled. Those features that cause a
large drop in the prediction performance can reversely be interpreted as the most important
for the prediction. However, when applying this method on the presented model, no feature
was standing out to be important as all results were very close to the baseline value. This
behaviour can be explained by two drawbacks of the feature permutation method. Firstly, the
shuffling is happening at random and so it is not known how similar the shuffled feature order
actually is to the initial order. This introduces a large uncertainty into the method, as important
features might be considered unimportant simply because the shuffling resulted in a similar
order to the initial one. And secondly, correlations between features can cause features to
seem unimportant, as their effect on the output might be covered by highly correlated features.
To address these problems, a different feature importance analysis is performed. Thereby, the
correlation between features is calculated, which tells us how much the change in one feature
can be explained by the change in another feature. The features are then clustered based on
their correlations and backward feature analysis is performed by omitting one feature cluster
and retraining the model. The evaluation is the same as for the feature permutation method,
such that features that cause the largest drop in the model performance after model retraining
can be considered the most important feature clusters for the model predictions.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
75
==PAGE:94==
The result of the feature permutation analysis as well an additional analysis of the feature im­
portance when using a simple random forest model are appended in Appendix A.5.
Section 4.1 outlined the different geographical aspects of each area. Considering that those
differences lead to varying environmental conditions that can influence the plankton population
in different ways, it is considered not feasible to assume that the same features are important
in all areas. Therefore, the analysis is performed separately for all three areas.
6.4.1
Feature Correlation Analysis
In order to find the correlation between the different features, they are first put into a dataframe
with each column representing one feature. The cloudcover is omitted from this analysis, given
it is just a binary mask and can thus not contribute to the correlation matrix. All rows containing
’NaN’ values are then removed from the dataframe. In order to investigate the temporal cor­
relation between the CHL values, the values of the previous 1 to 5 days are added as extra
features to the dataframe. Given that the data is not normally distributed, the Spearman’s rank
correlation coefficient is used for the evaluation, with ­1 indicating a strong negative correlation
and +1 a strong positive correlation. The Spearman rank correlation specifies if two variables
have a monotone relationship or not, including also non­linear monotone relationships. This is
done by first ranking the variables in relation to each other and then calculating the Pearson
correlation coefficient on this ranked data. Given two features F and E with ranks defined as
distinct integers, the correlation coefficient is calculated as:
rs = cov(R(Fi), R(Ei))
σR(Fi)σR(Ei)
= 1 −6 · (R(Fi) −R(Ei))2
n · (n2 −1)
(6.6)
where i is each individual observation, n is the total number of observations, R(Fi) is the rank
of feature F and R(Ei) is the rank of feature E.
In order to get a better understanding of possible correlated feature groups, hierarchical cluster­
ing using complete linkage clustering based on the correlation between features is performed.
Complete linkage clustering is an algorithm that sequentially clusters features together and con­
tinuously updates a distance matrix based on this clustering. Initially, every feature is put into
a single cluster and the distance matrix is the correlation matrix. Then, for each step in the
algorithm, the two feature clusters that are the closest together, based on the distance matrix,
are joined together. The distance matrix is updated by removing the two, now joined, feature
clusters and including a new entry for the new cluster. The distance between this cluster and the
other clusters is defined to be the furthest distance between either of the newly joined clusters
and all other clusters, like:
d(cnew(c1
old, c2
old), ci) = max{d(c1
old, ci), d(c2
old, ci)}
(6.7)
where d is the distance, cnew is the new cluster, c1
old the first joined clusters, c2
old the second
joined clusters and ci one of all other remaining clusters i. This procedure is repeated until
only one large cluster is left. The distance that caused features to be clustered together can be
visualized in a dendrogram, with smaller distances at the moment of clustering representing a
higher correlation between the clusters. [160]
Figures 6.20b, 6.21e and 6.20f show the correlation matrices and figures 6.20a, 6.21d and
6.20e the dendrograms of all features for the Gulf of Riga, Southern Kattegat and Gotland
Basin area respectively. It is clearly visible, that the previous days of CHL concentration have
the largest correlation with the current CHL concentration for all three areas. The influence of the
other features on the CHL concentration varies depending on the area. In the Gulf of Riga the
longitude has the largest positive correlation, indicating higher CHL concentration further east
76
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:95==
and the latitude has the largest negative correlation, indicating that plankton populations are
largest in the south. Based on the hierachical clustering, CHL forms a tight cluster with latitude
and a more distant cluster with topography. When grouping the features based on a distance of
0.8 between clusters, radiation and cosine of day of year are most correlated, with the smallest
distance between their clusters and are grouped in a bigger cluster with air temperature and
SST.
(a) Dendrogram, Gulf of Riga
(b) Correlation matrix, Gulf of Riga
(c) Dendrogram, Southern Kattegat
(d) Correlation matrix, Southern Kattegat
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
77
==PAGE:96==
(e) Dendrogram, Gotland Basin
(f) Correlation matrix, Gotland Basin
Figure 6.20: Correlation matrices and dendrograms of utilized features for the three subareas, Gulf of Riga, Southern
Kattegat and Gotland Basin
Furthermore, precipitation and wave height (Wave VHM0) form a cluster as well as the east­
west wind speed (Wind u10) and the wave direction (Wave VMDR). The relationship between
features in the Southern Kattegat is very similar to that of the Gulf of Riga, with one large cluster
made up of radiation, cosine of day of year, SST and air temperature, another cluster made up of
Wave VHM0 and precipitation as well as another cluster made up of Wind u10 and Wave VMDR.
The CHL concentration has the largest positive correlation with the topography, indicating that
more shallow areas host larger plankton populations. Those two features also form a cluster
together.
For the Gotland Basin the correlation between features shows some differences. While the
radiation and cosine of day of year still form a cluster and the air temperature and SST form a
cluster, these two clusters are seperated and the former cluster is combined with prescipitation
and the latter cluster is combined with the sine of day of year. Furthermore, Wind u10 forms
a cluster with Wave VHM0 and topography a cluster with longitude. The CHL concentration
does not show strong correlations to any of the other features and even the correlation to the
previous days of CHL concentration is weaker.
While the correlation and clustering analysis itself is not sufficient to analyze the importance of
the features for deep learning models, the analysis provides valuable insights into the feature
relationships that will be used in the following backward feature importance analysis and can
help to explain the final analysis results.
6.4.2
Backward Feature Importance Analysis
The backward feature importance analysis is performed based on the resulting clusters of the
correlation analysis. Given that highly correlated features can account for each other when indi­
vidually omitted from the training process, as the model is able to draw the relevant information
from the remaining feature, feature clusters are used for the analysis in this study. To form the
clusters, the dendrogram of the correlation analysis is used with a threshold of 0.8, resulting in
78
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:97==
8 feature clusters for each area. As previously mentioned those clusters are not necessarily the
same for each area. Table 6.14 shows an overview of the resulting clusters and their naming
convention for the purpose of the analysis.

Based on these groups, the model is retrained while omitting the features of each group. Then,
the RMSE value of the training set is calculated. Those feature groups that cause the RMSE
to increase are considered more important features, those that cause the RMSE value to drop
are reversely considered unimportant features. The performance is compared for each day
individually and then for all days together, resulting in a total feature importance.
Figure 6.21 shows a time series of the development of the RMSE with each feature group
omitted for each predicted day in the upper row. Those feature groups that lie above the baseline
can be interpreted as important feature groups, ranked by their height. Those that lie below the
baseline do not bring any value to the model and, on the opposite, rather decrease the model
performance by introducing noise and distracting the model. In the lower row, a ranked bar
chart is plotted, showing each individual feature, allocated the value of the associated feature
group. The features are coloured according to their associated group, as defined in Table 6.14.

creases over the duration of the predictions, thus the RMSE value increases, with the worst
