performance for the prediction of day 5. While the Gotland Basin and Gulf of Riga include
multiple features that lie above the baseline and thus bring value to the model and explain the
CHL concentration to a certain extend, for the Southern Kattegat region there is a significantly
larger drop in performance when the CHL concentration is omitted from the training, indicating
that the other features do not seem to account for the CHL concentration. As soon as the CHL
concentration is added to the model again, the performance increases drastically. Looking at the
total feature importance for each area individually, for the Gulf of Riga, Group 1, consisting of the
CHL concentration, the Cloudmask and the latitude, is the most important feature group for the
model. The second most important feature group is group 7, which is the longitude. The model
can thus explain a lot of the CHL concentration simply based on the position in the map, as
some areas consistently have larger values and other lower values. Group 2 includes temporal
values such as the Cosine of day of year, Radiation, Air Temperature and STT. Those features
also add value to the prediction, as does feature group 5, which includes the precipitation and
Wave VHM0. Group 4, Wind u10 and group 6, the topography also have a small impact on the
CHL prediction. Omitting group 8 and group 3 however, including Wind u10 and Wave VMDR
and Sine of day of year respectively, improves the model performance, indicating that these
features do not add any value to the predictions. For the Southern Kattegat, by far the most
important feature group is group 1, including the CHL concentration, cloudmask and topography
of the area. Additionally, group 5, including the precipitation and wave VHM0 also adds value
80
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:99==
to the model. By omitting all other feature groups, the model performance is improved, with
the best result when the Wind u10 and Wave VMDR are left out of the model training. This
behaviour suggests that for the Kattegat the CHL concentration can mainly be determined by
the topology and the concentration in the previous days, with some influence of the precipitation
and Wave VHM0.
In the Gotland Basin the largest influence can again be attributed to group 1, here only including
the CHL concentration and cloudmask. Yet also group 7, including the Wave VMDR and group
2, including the Sine of day, Air temperature and SST have a higher importance in relation to
the baseline results. The same can be observed for group 5, Wind u10 and Wave VHM0.
The immediate conclusion could be that excluding those feature groups for each area whose
permutation improved the model performance, should overall result in a higher performance of
the model. However, given the complexity of deep learning models, this must not necessarily
be the case, as the model might detect connections between the remaining features that are
beneficial for the model performance. The presented analysis therefore only provides a rough
estimate of what features are most important for the model. To perform feature elimination
based on this analysis, each combination would have to be tried to be permuted and the best
resulting model would move on to the next permutation level until the optimal setup is found.
Figure 6.22: Deviation from Baseline RMSE for each feature in the three areas
Looking at the difference between the feature importance in the three areas, Figure 6.22 shows
the anomaly from the baseline value for each of the three areas for each feature. Individual
features are allocated the values of their respective feature groups. The figure shows that
the CHL concentration and the associated cloud mask have the largest effect on the model
performance for all three areas. The other two features whose exclusion leads to an increase
in the RMSE value in all areas are the Wave VHM0 and the precipitation. All other features
influence the model output either negatively or positively, depending on the area. Based on
this observation no conclusion can be drawn as to which features are most important in the
whole Baltic Sea. Instead, the outcome emphasizes the need to locally define which features
are included into a deep learning model.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
81
==PAGE:100==
7
Discussion and Future Work
The efficiency of deep learning models to fill gaps in remote­sensing based spatio­temporal
data of CHL concentrations in the Baltic Sea and to make predictions based on that data was
investigated in this study. Through multiple experiments that compare different models and
preprocessing methods, a new setup to predict CHL concentrations in the Baltic Sea, using
deep learning models, was developed. In the two­step approach first the gaps in the data are
filled using the pCONV3D model and then predictions are performed using a ConvLSTM model
based on the gap­filled data. Furthermore, the most important features for the prediction were
analysed.
While gap­filling and prediction of image and especially satellite data are widely studied fields
of research, no previous work has applied deep learning models to fill gaps or predict CHL
concentrations in the Baltic Sea based on remote sensing data. This study thus constitutes a
starting point to understand if deep learning models can be effectively employed for the tasks
in this region and what further steps might be necessary to succeed.
In the following the gap­filling, prediction and feature analysis part of the study are individually
discussed, in order to address each research question.
7.1
Gap­Filling
The first research question asks for the effectiveness of deep learning models to fill data­gaps in
the spatio­temporal data of CHL concentrations. In the following, the steps that were followed
to answer this question are discussed, as well as the outcome of the analysis. Additionally
possible future work is outlined.
7.1.1
Discussion
Through extensive literature review, two recently presented deep learning models were chosen
to be applied on the selected datasets, in order to find the most suitable model to fill the data
gaps. Those models are the DINCAEv.2 model by Barth et al. [88] and the STConv3D model
by Appel [82]. Both models have an initial autoencoder U­Net structure that has previously
shown good results for image reconstruction tasks. The unique characteristic of the DINCAE
model is the inclusion of additional features such as sine and cosine of the day of year, the
normalized latitude and longitude and the CHL concentrations of the previous and following
days. Furthermore, the model has a refined model architecture, that consists of two stacked
U­Nets with two dimensional convolutions and optimizes the log­likelihood based on the output
of both, the intermediate and final U­Net defined by a ratio. The characteristics that stands out
in the STConv3D model are three dimensional partial convolutions, that allow the model to fill
gaps for multiple days at a time while the partial convolutions allow the model to focus only on
the available data. For this study the STConv3D model was modified to have additional layers
in the encoder and decoder layer in comparison to the original model. This deeper setup allows
the model to capture more features from the provided data. The resulting model is referred
to as pCONV3D and optimizes the mean average error based on predefined validation pixels.
In an attempt to combine the unique aspects of both models, furthermore a third model called
DINpCAE was developed, that inherits the architecture of the DINCAE model and includes the
partial convolutions that were present in the STConv3D model into the U­Net structure of the
first U­Net. Furthermore, a kernel stride of 2 is used in the DINpCAE model for dimensionality
reduction.
82
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:101==
In contrast to single­source model setups that are used to fill data gaps in satellite images, such
as the CloudGAN by Schonenberg and Kluiver [61], the selected models utilize spatio­temporal
information to fill the gaps, enabling to fill gaps for entire days without data. In comparison to
other hybrid methods, such as the STGAN by Sarukkai et al. [77], that utilizes RGB and infrared
bands of the satellite data to first detect the clouds and consequently fill the gaps, the model
setups are simplified and focused just on the gap­filling task.
Given the data sparsity with approximately 70% of the data missing, it was impossible to have
sufficient training days with full data coverage to assess the gap­filling skills of the model. There­
fore, artificial validation gaps were generated using perlin noise, that has shown good results
to generate artificial clouds in previous studies [151], to dynamically omit from 5 to 95% of the
available data during training and 20% of the data during validation.
Initially the Gulf of Riga was used as the main research area and all models were trained based
on 20­day time blocks using different data transformations, data availabilities, datasets and
gap sizes. The experiments showed that a minimum data availability is crucial for the model
performance. Even though the total number of training data decreased when omitting data with
lower pixel availability, the model performance increased until a certain turning point. Model
training with at least 30% of pixel availability showed superior results for the DINpCAE and
pCONV3D model, while the DINCAE model performed best with 20% or more data available.
Furthermore, log transformation of the data showed increased performance for the pCONV3D
and DINpCAE model, while min­max normalization worked best for the DINCAE model. All
models performed better when using both available datasets, the MULTI and OLCI dataset.
The pCONV3D outperformed the DINCAE and DINpCAE model significantly in all experiments
when considering the RMSE value and was thus considered the best model to be applied on
all three areas. As a possible cause for the low performance of the DINCAE and DINpCAE
models, an inconsistency in the preprocessing steps of the models was found. Prior to passing
the data to the model, the mean of each 20­day time block was subtracted from the training
data and later again added to the predictions. However, the model did not know the scale of
this individual mean and thus the procedure introduced a large uncertainty to the process that
hindered the model to account for the final data distribution. The superior results of the DINpCAE
model compared to the DINCAE model indicate that the additional partial convolutions enhance
model performance. However, further studies are necessary to draw a definitive conclusion on
the effectiveness of partial convolutions and the overall efficiency of the model on the provided
dataset. These studies could involve using either the overall mean of the entire dataset or
excluding this preprocessing step altogether.
Optimizing the model only for the Gulf of Riga and applying it for the Gotland Basin and Kattegat
areas resulted in a drop in performance for the latter two areas. This behaviour indicates that
the large local differences, outlined in Section 2.1 and 4.1, between areas in the Baltic Sea
urge the need to optimize individual models for each area or include information from all areas
in a global model. This is especially relevant for coastal and open­water areas. The model
that was optimized for the Gulf of Riga showed a better transferability to the Southern Kattegat
region, both of them being coastal areas, than to the Gotland Basin, an open­water area. This is
emphasized by the comparison of the deep learning model to common interpolation techniques.
While the deep learning model outperformed all interpolation techniques for the Gulf of Riga,
OK showed lower RMSE values in the Southern Kattegat and performed best overall in the
Gotland Basin.
This might lead to the conclusion, that the deep learning model is not effective in filling the
gaps in the CHL concentrations. However, effectiveness must not necessarily only refer to the
numerical results. The proposed deep learning model has two severe advantages over the OK
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
83
==PAGE:102==
interpolation method that must be included into the final analysis. Firstly, it is significantly faster
in filling the gaps, once the model is trained and secondly, it is capable of filling gaps in the
data also for days that have absolutely no data available. Taking that into account, the model
represents a fast and reliable alternative to common interpolation techniques to perform the
gap­filling, especially when model parameters are optimized for the area.
7.1.2
Future Work
Overall, the experiments on the gap­filling model showed, that while the model is able to capture
the rough distribution of the data in all three regions, it struggles to reconstruct the whole diversity
of the data and focuses strongly on values around the mean. This behaviour can be attributed
to several different reasons that leave room for further research and discussion.
In the selected approach the data was split into 20­day time blocks for training. This reduced the
available number of training samples, potentially impacting model performance. Deep learning
models thrive on more data, so in this study, the MULTI and OLCI datasets were combined to
increase the available data. Nevertheless, the final model trained on 20­day blocks with over
30% data availability, had only 161 blocks in total: 123 from MULTI and 38 from OLCI. Despite
the dataset’s imbalance, Experiment 1.4 (Section 6.2.6) showed that including both dataset im­
proved the model performance. However, the limited data availability could explain why the
model struggled to accurately reconstruct the overall trend for OLCI, resulting in smaller R2 val­
ues. To generate more training samples, different approaches can be explored. One approach
is to experiment with smaller block sizes for model input. Using 10­day blocks instead of 20­day
blocks could double the number of available training blocks. Another approach is data augmen­
tation, which involves modifying the input data by flipping blocks or applying filters. However,
caution is necessary to avoid overfitting and preserve the model’s ability to capture geographi­
cal trends. Alternatively, a rolling window scheme could be applied, overlapping input blocks to
generate additional training data. It’s crucial to prevent data leakage, such as by designating
separate months or seasons as test data. In general, it can be expected that generating more
training data will result in a better model performance, yet the performance should be carefully
monitored in order to avoid overfitting.
Another disadvantage of limited data availability is the scarcity of testing samples. These sam­
ples are crucial for evaluating the model’s performance on unseen data and assessing its gen­
eralization ability. However, when the test sample size is small, there is a higher likelihood
that they do not accurately represent the overall data distribution. This raises doubts about
whether the model’s performance on this subset is representative of its performance on unseen
data in general, as the test results may be influenced by noise present in the limited samples.
The presented approach is particularly susceptible to this uncertainty due to both the small test
dataset, consisting of 32 20­day blocks (23 MULTI and 9 OLCI), and the validation method that
uses only a subset of the data. To enhance result reliability, one possible approach is to employ
cross­validation. This involves dividing the data into different subsets and training the model on
all possible combinations, reserving one subset for validation each time. The results are col­
lected and aggregated for the final assessment of the model. While cross­validation provides
a more robust estimate of model performance, it is time­consuming due to the lengthy training
times of the model. Alternatively, the model could be tested by generating different validation
masks for the same image and filling the gaps multiple times, aggregating the results for the
final assessment of the model.
To successfully apply deep learning models, next to the data availability also the data quality is a
crucial factor. Therefore, to continue this work, before investigating further deep learning archi­
tectures, a focus should be laid on the data cleaning and preprocessing steps. In this study, the
decision was made to keep all outliers in the data, considering plankton blooms are rare events
84
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:103==
that can be seen as outliers to some extent. However, it might be worth exploring whether
model performance can be enhanced by filtering out extreme outliers while still preserving the
ability to detect plankton blooms. Furthermore, significant outliers in form of patches with very
low values during bloom times were detected in the data for the Gulf of Riga and Gotland Basin
area. Investigating the source of these outliers and devising a reliable data cleaning process to
eliminate such values is important. While for the Gulf of Riga a simple threshold of 1 was effec­
tive in removing all data with lower values, this method cannot be applied in the Gotland Basin,
due to the lower CHL concentrations during non­bloom times. Moreover, the optical complexity
of the Baltic Sea leads to a high noise level in the data products, especially for the OLCI dataset.
This variability makes it challenging for deep learning models to extract reliable, general fea­
tures. To mitigate the impact of noise on the training outcomes, exploring different smoothing
methods for initializing the model training could be beneficial. Previous studies have shown
that initializing convolutional models with a Gabor filter, for example, can potentially improve
model performance [161]. Alternatively, data smoothing prior to training could be considered,
where the target output is also a smoothed dataset. However, this procedure should be carefully
monitored and assessed to make sure that no relevant information is lost during the smoothing
process.
An entirely different approach would be to include information of the different bands of the satel­
lites to the training data, that can possibly help the model to recognize the cause for the differ­
ences in the data and thus improve the overall performance. However, this would result in more
model parameters and thus a higher model complexity which again increases the necessary
computational power and training time.
Looking at the disparity between the different areas, the study shows that the model perfor­
mance is highly dependent on the associated area and optimizing the model for one area is
not enough to show sufficient results in another area. Thereby it must be especially separated
between coastal and open­water areas. To overcome this problem a possible solution could be
to modify the model architecture and train the model all areas at once, possibly indicating the
associated area again with a additional categorical channel or using a patching method com­
parable to the one introduced by Ye et al. [109]. Such an improved model setup can possibly
help to mitigate the differences in model performance between the areas and help the model to
generalize better.
7.2
Prediction
The second research question asks if deep learning models are able to predict CHL concen­
trations more accurately than existing biogeochemical models. The following section discusses
the process that was undertaken to answer this question and outlines possible future work to
improve and manifest the results.
7.2.1
Discussion
Based on previous studies for time series prediction of spatio­temporal data, two different deep
learning models were constructed in order to find a suitable model to perform predictions of the
CHL concentrations. Both models are based on convolutional LSTM models [36]. The advan­
tage of the convolutional LSTM layers is that spatial information is integrated into the recursive
LSTM model and thus features in the spatial as well temporal dimension can be extracted. Due
to the recursiveness of the LSTM model, the number of features in the model easily scale up
when building complex model architectures, leading to very deep models with a lot of learnable
parameters. While possibly being more powerful for large datasets, such highly complex models
tend to overfit easily when having less training data available. Due to the previously mentioned
sparse data availability, this study therefore employs a simpler setup of three stacked layers for
both models. The first model stacks three ConvLSTM layers, followed by a 3D convolutional
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
85
==PAGE:104==
layer. The second model also applied three stacked recursive layers and a final 3D convolu­
tional 3D layer, yet applying a self­attention mechanism as part of the ConvLSTM layer [111].
A general advantage of recursive deep learning models is their capacity to extract long­term
temporal relationships from the data. However, due to the reoccurring datagaps in the winter
months and the ambiguity of the gap­filling results, this characteristic could not be exploited
in the project. Therefore, instead of passing the entire time series to the model, the data was
again split into blocks of 20 days and a rolling window scheme was employed on these blocks,
generating datasets that include 10 historical days to predict 5 days ahead. This number of
historic days was defined based on visual inspection of the datasets, that showed a rather
static behaviour of the data for smaller historical timeframes, while providing some diversity
when using 10 days, which can help the model to capture the temporal development. The
number of days that are predicted were defined based on the data availability and the amount
of days that the numerical predictions forecast. Furthermore, additional features were defined
based on literature suggestions and company­intern domain knowledge, that supplement the
CHL concentration data.
The experiments show that the model employing simple ConvLSTM layers performs better with
log transformed data, while the Sa­ConvLSTM model shows the best results using min­max
normalization. While the hyperparameter optimization worked sufficiently for the ConvLSTM
and improved the overall model performance, the process failed for the Sa­ConvLSTM model.
A possible explanation for this behaviour is the previously mentioned risk of overfitting when
using more complex models. The Sa­ConvLSTM model has significantly more parameters than
the simple ConvLSTM model. While this can enable the model to extract more defined features,
it also increases the risk that the model overfits to the specific training data, especially given
the comparatively small dataset. Furthermore, the optimization process was applied on the
produced validation dataset. This dataset only consisted of 5 individual datasets, resulting in
30 datasets after applying the rolling window scheme. Most likely, the Sa­ConvLSTM model
adapted the parameters to optimally fit the training dataset, given the provided validation dataset
and therefore was not able to generalize to the unknown test dataset. For that reason the model
was considered not suitable for the given problem and the ConvLSTM model was applied on
all three research areas.
As in the gap­filling process, the model was optimized for the Gulf of Riga and applied on the
Southern Kattegat and Gotland Basin area. A drop in performance could be observed when
applying the model on the additional two areas based on the NRMSE value. Interestingly,
the NRMSE value was the worst for the Southern Kattegat compared to the other two areas,
indicating that the model does not manage to capture the data nuances and complexities that
are present in that area, yet the R2 value was the best, explaining most of the variance in the
data. On the opposite, the Gotland Basin while showing low R2 values, the NRMSE value
was considerably better. These results emphasize again the importance to fine­tune the model
for the different environmental conditions in each area. While for the gap­filling the biggest
discrepancy could be observed between coastal and open­water areas, for the prediction the
water mixing rate seems to play a large role. While the Southern Kattegat lies at the inflow
channel with water from the North Sea, the Gotland Basin and Gulf of Riga have comparably low
mixing rates, as explained in Section 2.1. This could lead to easier predictability for these areas.
This assumption is emphasized when comparing the model output to two naive baseline models.
While the deep learning model performs best for the Gulf of Riga and Gotland Basin, the baseline
method of simply predicting the temporal mean of the data outperforms the model prediction
when regarding the RMSE value. This implies that the model makes wrong assumptions based
on the data, that actually hinder the overall model performance.
86
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:105==
Compared to the numerical model, the presented method exhibits superior performance when
validated against in­situ data. The ConvLSTM model demonstrated an enhanced ability to cap­
ture the underlying distribution, as evidenced by its smaller RMSE values and larger R2 values,
which indicate its capacity to represent the overall trend. Nevertheless, it is important to be
aware of the limitations that are associated with the in­situ dataset that was utilized for this
comparison. The data comprises a small sample size, with measurements of only 4 days within
a 9­day time span and encompassing only a restricted region of the Gotland Basin. It is therefore
questionable if the result is representative for the overall performance of the model. Further­
more, measurements can be subject to errors that occur when collecting the data. For example,
plankton show a special behaviour called quenching when they are exposed to sunlight, which
reduces the amount of fluorescence that the plant transmits which can reduce the measurement
accuracy. Each measurement instrument must be carefully calibrated to the given conditions
in order to correct those natural phenomena, yet some error must still be accounted for [162].
Additionally, water samples can be very variable depending on where exactly the sample was
taken. By binning the measurements into a congruent grid structure with the ConvLSTM predic­
tions as it was done in this study, the effect of individual measurement errors can be reduced.
Yet it is important to acknowledge that some level of uncertainty will still be present in the in­situ
data.
When comparing the numerical model forecasts and the ConvLSTM predictions a large dis­
similarity between both approaches was observed. Overall, the values of the numerical model
forecasts were considerably smaller than those predicted by the ConvLSTM model. This phe­
nomenon was already mentioned in the quality report of the numerical model product. However,
in some cases especially in the Southern Kattegat, the values of the numerical model substan­
tially surpassed those of the ConvLSTM model. A possible reason for this observation could
be found when looking at an example image of both models. While the numerical model pre­
dicted more defined structures, those did not align with the dynamics that were captured in the
satellite imagery and thus reconstructed by the ConvLSTM model. There are several explana­
tions that could explain this behaviour. First of all, the satellite images are already estimations
of the CHL concentrations, extracted by complex atmospheric correction algorithms. Due to
the optical complexity of the Baltic Sea it is possible, that the dynamics that are visible in the
satellite CHL imagery do not correspond to the actual plankton distribution, as possibly other
substances that were floating in the water were considered plankton by the algorithms. Another
explanation originates in the setup of the numerical model. As explained in Section 2.2, the
model is based on interactions between many variables in an ecosystem, that are represented
by mathematical formulas using different parameters. It is thus a large­scale simplification of
very complex systems and it is possible, that the plankton in the real world behaves differently
than calculated by the numerical model.
Overall, the study shows that by using the presented method, the model is able to compete with
the existing numerical model when making predictions of CHL concentrations. Compared to in­
situ samples that were used for validation, the model was capable to outperform the numerical
predictions for all five days in advance.
7.2.2
Future Work
Based on the outlined results, deep learning models are viable alternatives to numerical models
for making predictions of CHL concentration in the Baltic Sea. However, also multiple drawbacks
and downsides of the approach were mentioned, that require further investigation and research
before the model can be confidently used to make reliable predictions.
As previously mentioned, a general advantage of recursive deep learning models is their ca­
pacity to extract long­term temporal relationships from the data. However, in this study this
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
87
==PAGE:106==
characteristic was not exploited, as only 20­day blocks were passed to the model at once. A
possible method to make more use of the recursive advantage, could be to only exclude those
time blocks that have absolutely no data present and adapt the model to accept blocks with
variable time durations. This way the model might be better able to learn the temporal charac­
teristics directly from the data and extract more temporal features. Another advantage of that
method could be, that by using larger data blocks more training data could be generated when
using the rolling window scheme. Currently only 20­day blocks with more than 30% data avail­
ability were used to train the prediction model. However, it is possible that the model would
perform better when using data also with a lower initial pixel availability, as it would increase
the amount of training data. Doing a sensitivity analysis with varying data availability in the
training data on the current model can also be a useful first step to investigate if using the pre­
viously mentioned method of only omitting times with absolutely no data over a certain duration
is feasible.
The current model architecture consists simply of three stacked ConvLSTM layers and a final
Conv3D layer, performing a three dimensional convolution to predict all five days at once. Alter­
native setups to this model could be a hybrid approach, where the output of each day’s prediction
is passed to an additional ConvLSTM or Conv3D layer, that thus gets more recent information,
before returning the predictions of all days. In a brief experiment during this study, appended in
Appendix A.4.2, this setup showed superior results to the current ConvLSTM setup, yet inher­
ited a high risk of getting stuck in local minima during the optimization process. Due to the time
constraints in this project, the model setup was thus not further investigated. However, possible
ways to escape the local minima can be to use a different optimizer such as stochastic gradi­
ent decent instead of the utilized Adam optimizer, and training the model for a larger number
of epochs. An alternative approach could be to alternate the learning rate during the training
process, in order to let the model escape local minima. When identifying a method to overcome
the local minima problem, the hybrid setup could be a reasonable way to improve the overall
model performance.
Another hyperparameter that was not explored in this study is the time span that is passed
to the model in order to make the prediction. While it was defined that 10 days were used to
predict the following 5 days, it is possible that more or less days actually yield to a better model
performance. Doing a sensitivity analysis on this parameter could thus also lead to a better
understanding of the model performance.
7.3
Feature Analysis and Selection
The third research question asks for the features that are most important for deep learning
models to predict CHL concentrations. The following discussion targets the process that was
followed to answer this question and shows following steps to build upon the presented work.
7.3.1
Discussion
The presented study shows that given the diversity of the Baltic Sea, it is not possible to gener­
alize over features for each area, but it is necessary to include local variations. However, while
different features can enhance the model performance in each area, the CHL data is the most
important feature to make predictions of future CHL concentrations. In order to find the most
important features for each area, first feature permutation was performed by randomly shuffling
one feature at a time and making predictions with this feature shuffled. However, this method
did not bring any meaningful results, as all features seemed unimportant. Therefore, a differ­
ent two­step approach was followed, based on clustering by correlation and backward feature
analysis.
In this approach, first the correlation between each feature pair was calculated using the Spear­
88
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:107==
man correlation and based on this correlation, the features were clustered using hierachical
clustering. The clustering groups features together, based on what feature clusters are closest
together in a distance matrix that is constantly updated until all features are in one big cluster.
In order to get the individual clusters, a threshold can be defined based on the distance. Due to
a low correlation between most of the features in this study a threshold of 0.8 was defined, with
features that are closer together being clustered together. However, a distance of 0.8 is still
rather far and it is possible that investigating the individual effect of these features might have
shown different results. Furthermore, the clusters depend on the different clustering method
that was used. Applying a different cluster method might have led to different clusters and thus
to different results overall.
In the backward feature analysis, each cluster was omitted from the model training once, and the
drop in performance was analysed. Clusters that caused the model performance to drop more
were considered more important. Using this approach showed, that for all areas the previous
CHL concentration is the most important feature for future CHLconcentration prediction. For
coastal areas, such as the Gulf of Riga and Southern Kattegat, geographical properties such
as latitude, longitude and topography are additionally very important, while for the open water
area, Gotland Basin, omitting those parameters leads to an improvement in the model perfor­
mance. Overall, the coastal areas had very similar clusters. Yet while in the Gulf of Riga the
model performance dropped when omitting most features, in the Southern Kattegat the model
performance actually improved. Furthermore, the effect of the cluster that included the CHL
concentration and topography was significantly more important than in the other areas.
7.3.2
Future Work
Overall, the analysis showed that it is not possible to define uniform features for the whole
Baltic Sea. The feature importance very much depends on the individual area and thus these
difference should be accounted for when making predictions.
One feature group that was entirely omitted in this study are chemical components, such as
nutrient concentrations, salinity and pH value of the water. The reason for this is that the only
data source for nutrient concentration would be the numerical model that was also used to
evaluate the model performance. To avoid a large dependence of the deep learning model on
this specific numerical model, the decision was made to omit the nutrient concentration from the
analysis. However, previous studies have shown that plankton populations are highly correlated
to nutrient loads in the water. Therefore, it would be interesting to add the nutrient load of the
water in future work to access the influence of that feature on the model performance of the
deep learning model.
An alternative approach to using the actual features as input to the model is to use feature
extraction beforehand, and passing the extracted features to the model. Lee et al. [163] have
shown good results for CHL prediction using the HOPLS feature extraction for one dimensional
timeseries of river CHL. Expanding their approach to extract features from the two dimensional
data space used in this study, the approach can possibly enhance the model performance.
Another alternative would be to calculate the shapely values. Shapely values are a method
that originally comes from game theory and are now used to make machine and deep learning
models more explainable. The objective is to split the payoff of the model, which in the case of a
deep learning model is the prediction, equally amongst the contributors, which are the features,
according to how much a feature contributed to the outcome.
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
89
==PAGE:108==
8
Conclusion
The presented study demonstrates that deep learning models have the potential to be effec­
tively employed to fill data­gaps and predict chlorophyll­a concentrations to estimate plankton
populations in the Baltic Sea. Although the observed results vary amongst the three subareas
that were chosen to represent the whole region, the utilized models achieved superior results
compared to traditional methods in the Gulf of Riga, the region used for model optimization.
Furthermore, the model predictions were closer to in­situ samples than those of existing bio­
chemical models, showing the ability of deep learning models for more accurate predictions.
The study used the OLCI L3 and MULTI dataset from the Copernicus Marine Data Store to opti­
mize models for the Gulf of Riga area before generalizing to the Southern Kattegat and Gotland
Basin. Based on this data, a three dimensional convolutional U­Net architecture, pCONV3D,
was identified as the best model for gap­filling, outperforming common interpolation techniques
in the Gulf of Riga, yet being outperformed in the Gotland Basin. For prediction, that included
additional features such as the sea surface temperature, a ConvLSTM model with a final con­
volutional layer achieved the best results, outperforming naive and historical average baselines
in the Gulf of Riga and Gotland Basin, yet not in the Southern Kattegat. Suggested explana­
tions for these variations include the geographical and physical differences amongst the areas,
which may make it insufficient to optimize models just for one area. This was mirrored in the
varying results of feature importance across different areas. Furthermore, the low data quality
with different noise levels in different areas, as well as limitations in the study design, leading
to low sample sizes, may hinder the model to generalize to different areas.
Despite these disparities, evaluating the model performance against in­situ and numerical model
outputs in the Gotland Basin, showed a superior performance for the deep learning model, drop­
ping the root mean square error by 72%. This leads to the conclusions that (1) deep learning
models can effectively fill gaps in the spatio­temporal data of chlorophyll­a concentrations, given
the model is optimized for the region, taking local variation into account, (2) the feature impor­
tance varies across different areas, with temporal features having a higher influence in the Gulf
of Riga and Gotland Basin, while geographical features are more influential in the Southern Kat­
tegat and (3) deep learning models can predict chlorophyll­a concentrations and thus quantify
plankton populations in the Baltic Sea more accurately than existing biogeochemical models
based on the comparison to in­situ samples.
Overall, the study highlights the potential of deep learning models as a fast and reliable alter­
native to existing methods and opens up new possibilities for accurately filling gaps in remote
sensing data and predicting plankton populations in the Baltic Sea. By extracting non­linear
feature relations from historical data, deep learning models are less dependent on assump­
tions of ecosystem interactions, leading to less errors. However, local differences in model
optimization and feature selection must be considered. The study also emphasises the need
to perform further research on improving the data quality and exploring alternative data pre­
processing methods, as well as evaluating model performance with in­situ samples in a larger
scale.
By investigating and outlining the potential of deep learning models to quantify plankton popula­
tions in the Baltic Sea, this study contributes to the ongoing efforts of mitigating climate change
effects. With ongoing research in improving data quality and preprocessing methods, it provides
a foundation that can be extended to enable the integration into numerical model forecasts, em­
powering proactive measures to reduce the impact of plankton blooms in the Baltic Sea.

A.1.1
MLP­ENS Algorithm
The MLP­ENS algorithm was introduced by Brando et al. [43], to address the problems asso­
ciated with the optical complexity in the Baltic Sea. It obtains considerably better results than
previous methods when compared to in­situ data. Therefore, this algorithms was integrated
into the CMEMS Ocean Color products (MULTI and OLCI L3) for the Baltic Sea by Coperni­
cus Marine Data Service that provide the base for the presented project. As the name already
suggests, the algorithm consists of two parts, a multilayer perceptron (MLP) and a ensemble
scheme (ENS). The MLP algorithm consists of one hidden layer with ten neurons and is trained
using a scaled conjugate gradient with a weight decay regularization coefficient of 0.05. The
input Rrs values are first log­transformed and then z­score scaled using the mean (µl) and stan­
dard deviation (σl) of the log­transformed Rrs values for the MLP training. The MLP function is
defined as y = f(x), where the input vector x had entries xi for i = 1, ..., nλ, with nλ indicating
the number of input Rrs wavelengths. The value of the hidden unit j, denoted aj, is a linear
combination of the input quantities:
aj = b(1)j +
nλ
X
i=1
w(1)jixi
Here, w(1)ji represents the weight linking the input unit i to the hidden unit j, and b(1)j is the
bias adaptive parameter. The activation of the hidden unit j, identified with zj and representing
the input for the next layer, is obtained using the hyperbolic tangent activation function:
zj = g(aj) = eaj −e−aj
eaj + e−aj
Then, output y is computed as:
y = b(2) +
M
X
i=1
w(2)jzj
With M indicating the number of hidden units with weight w(2)j, and b(2) the bias coefficient.
Finally, the MLP output is back­transformed to the chlorophyll­a concentration as
Chl −aMLP = 10(y · σc + µc)
.
To apply the ensemble scheme and combine the data of the multiple sensors, first a novelty
index is calculated based on the eigenvectors (Aν) and eigenvalues (γν) from the principal
component analysis of the log­transformed Rrs data, resulting in:
Thus, the final target data is calculated by:
Chl −aENS =
PN
i=1
1
νi · Chl −aMLPi
PN
i=1
1
νi
. [43]

A.1.2
Alternative Atmospheric Correction Algorithm
The alternative atmospheric correction algorithm is applied by EUMETSAT to generate the OLCI
L2 CHL data. The algorithm consists of two neural networks, an inverse and a forward neural
network, where the output of the first neural network is the input to the second neural network.
The first neural network is an inverse neural network with four hidden layers, that estimates
the concentrations of the water constituents, including phytoplankton and suspended matter. It
has as input the top of atmosphere (TOA) reflectances in 15 bands (400­753 nm, 778, 865 and
1020 nm), corrected for absorbing gases and the smile effect (a spectral shift of the sensor),
wind speed, salinity, temperature, altitude of the water surface and observation geometry [164].
The second neural network is a forward neural network, that takes the output of the first neural
network, together with the other properties and estimates the reflectances. The reflectances
are then compared to the measured reflectances to access the quality of the retrieval. This
procedure is necessary, as the training data is based on simulated data from a bio­optical model,
which relates scattering and absorption coefficients to concentrations.
The concentrations are then transformed into the three outputs: scattering of all particles bbp (1),
absorption of phytoplankton pigments apig (2) and absorption of coloured detrital and dissolved
material adetritus + ag(3). Absorption (1) and (2) are then recalculated into concentrations of the
water constituent, which leads to the estimated CHL concentration of:
CHL_NN = 21a1.04
pig
The conversion is based on measurements in the Southern North Sea as explained by Doerffer
et al. [134].

Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea

A.1.3
Data Exploration
Figure A.1 shows values of single pixels over time in each area. The figure shows time series
for two individual pixels in each of the three subareas for all three datasets. In the Southern
Kattegat and Gotland Basin region, the OLCI L2 dataset is visibly more noisy, especially during
the start and end of the year. In the Gulf of Riga however, a more brackish water, the MULTI
and OLCI L3 datasets seem to show more noisy behaviour than the OLCi L2 dataset.
Figure A.1: Comparison of single pixel values during 2021 for two pixels in each area of each datasets
A.1.4
Correspondence with Copernicus Marine Service
Due to inconsistencies in the different data products, a correspondence with Copernicus Marine
Data Service was initiated, in order to understand the source of the differences between the
internal MULTI and OLCI L3 data products as well as the significant differences between their
OLCI L3 product and the OLCI L2 product provided by EUMETSAT.
• Question regarding the difference between the MULTI and the OLCI product provided by
the Copernicus Marine Data Service
Answer (Quote): The difference between OLCI and MULTI is a well known problem and
it originates from:
1. difference Rrs at any of the bands used for the Chl calculation will cause different
Chl values
2. OLCI merges S3A and B processed by POLYMER at 300 m
3. MULTI CCIv6 merges AQUA, VIIRS, VIIRSJ and OLCI processed with specific AC
(although also based on POLYMER) at 1 km
• Question regarding the difference between the OLCI L2 product by EMEUSAT and the
OLCI L3 product provided by the Copernicus Marine Data Service
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
105
==PAGE:124==
Answer (Quote): For the OLCI datasets, we don’t use the standard L2 product (in­
cluding CHL_NN or CHL_OC4ME). We start from the L1b images, which are processed
using POLYMER to obtain the Rrs (that are different from the Rrs available in the
standard L2 product), merged in the L3 reflectance dataset (cmems_obs­oc_bal_bgc­
reflectance_my_l3­olci­300m_P1D). Chl­a are computed from the POLYMER Rrs using
the method described in Brando et al. [43] and then merged in the L3 plankton dataset
(cmems_obs­oc_bal_bgc­plankton_my_l3­olci­300m_P1D).

A.3.2
Area Loss Function Analysis
Analysing the loss development during training can help to assess the model performance.
Ideally, the model converges to a stable value with increasing numbers of epochs.
To assess the model performance of the pCONV3D model for gap­filling of the three different
areas (Gulf of Riga, Southern Kattegat and Gotland Basin), the development of the loss function
during training is visualized in Figure A.26, separately for the training and validation dataset.
(a) Gulf of Riga
(b) Southern Kattegat
(c) Gotland Basin
Figure A.26: Loss development during training for the three subareas, pCONV3D
The figure shows that overall, the model does not over­fit to the training data in any of the areas.
The smoothest convergence happens in the Southern Kattegat, where also the lowest loss value
is achieved. In the Gulf of Riga more variation in the validation loss can be observed, which
however stabilizes towards the end of the training. In the Gotland Basin, the model struggles
initially to reduce the loss value, however converges to a stable value over time, however the
overall loss is not significantly reduced.
Reasons for the increased loss in the training dataset compared to the valiation dataset are reg­
ularization techniques that add additional loss to the training value. Overall, the model shows a
good convergence behaviour for all areas. The loss development in the Gotland Basin indicates,
that the model setup could be optimized for that area in order to achieve a higher performance,
which aligns with the findings in Section 6.2.7.
122
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:141==
A.3.3
Interpolation Methods
The following interpolation methods were used to assess the performance of the gap­filling
models.
Nearest Neighbor
The concept of nearest neighbor interpolation is, that single pixels are similar to those surround­
ing the pixel. If a pixel value is missing, it is estimated based on the closest nearby known pixels.
In the simplest version, the nearest neighbor interpolation assigns the unknown pixel simply the
value of the spatially closest pixel. However, some variations of nearest neighbor interpolation
exist, that take into account a number of nearby pixels. In the project, the 5 closest pixels are
chosen and their euclidean distance to the unknown pixel is used as weights to calculate the
new value as a weighted mean of the known values. By considering multiple nearest neighbors
and incorporating their distances as weights, this enhanced approach takes into account the
proximity and influence of neighboring points in the estimation process [165], [155].
2D Kriging
In 2D kriging the spatial autocorrelation between known pixels is used to impute the missing
pixels. It is a two step process, where first a variogram is fitted to the known pixels to determine
the spatial covariance structure. For the variogram, the distance and similarity between every
pair of pixels is calculated. Therefore, it includes information about the spatial dependency
of the data. In the second step, weights are calculated based on the spatial location of the
unknown pixel in reference to the known pixels. The weights are derived from the covariance
structure and are used to interpolate the unknown pixels like
ˆz(x0) =
N
X
i=1
λiz(xi)
where ˆz, the unknown pixel at location x0 is equal to the sum of all known pixels z at their
locations, times the weight that is assigned to that location. In the project the simplest version
of kriging, Ordinary kriging is applied to compare the model performance [54], [52], [166].

A.4.2
Parallel Model Output
The experiments showed that the model struggles to capture individual dynamics between the
five output days. This behaviour can possibly be attributed to the final layer of the model, that is
set to be a 3D convolutional layer with 5 features, assuming a strong spatial correlation between
the days. In an approach to optimize the model performance this final layer is exchanged by 5
parallel layers, one for each day. By applying an individual convolution on each day, the idea is
that the model might be able to better capture the individual dynamics for each day, based on
the provided features. The updated architecture is illustrated in Figure A.29. Predicting each
day separately also results in a different loss function. While for the previous architecture the
loss function is calculated as the mean MSE over all values for all days, the MSE for the parallel
output is the sum of the mean MSE for each individual day.
Lparallel(x, y) =
D
X
d=1
 PNd
i=1(xi −yi)2
Nd
!
(A.1)
Where D is the number of days that are predicted, Nd is the total number of pixels at day d, x
is the observed variable and y is the predicted variable.
Figure A.29: Model architecture of ConvLSTM model with parallel output
While some trainings showed superior results to the initial model architecture, as shown in Table
A.5 it could be observed, that the model gets easily stuck in local minima, optimizing weights
to improve only the performance of some days while ignoring other days. This can be seen in
the high total loss that results from training the parallel­output model when getting stuck in a
local minima compared to the parallel­output model during a successful training run as well as
in visual inspections as shown in Figure A.30.

A.4.4
Area Loss Function Analysis
Analysing the loss development during training can help to assess the model performance.
Ideally, the model converges to a stable value with increasing numbers of epochs.
To assess the model performance on the three different areas (Gulf of Riga, Southern Kattegat
and Gotland Basin) for the prediction using the ConvLSTM model, the development of the loss
function during training is visualized in Figure A.33, seperately for the training and validation
dataset.
(a) Gulf of Riga
(b) Southern Kattegat
(c) Gotland Basin
Figure A.33: Loss development during training for the three subareas, ConvLSTM model
The figure shows a steady conversion of the loss function for all areas. Interestingly, in the
Southern Kattegat the validation loss is lower than the training loss. Reasons for this behaviour
can be regularization techniques that add penalties to the loss function of the training dataset
and thus increase the loss compared to the validation dataset. However, given that it only occurs
in the Southern Kattegat, the cause lies most likely in the data itself.
From Section 6.3.5 it is known that ultimately, the model performs is worse in the Southern
Kattegat than in the other two areas. A possible explanation is therefore the relatively small
validation dataset, that might not be representative for the whole dataset, due to random fluc­
tuations that specifically favor the validation set during training. Reversely the same can be the
case for the testing dataset.
In the Gotland Basin a relatively large difference between the validation and test dataset can
be seen. This could indicate that the model slightly overfits to the training dataset in that area.
Overall, the insights that the loss function analysis provide align with the findings of the model
performance in Section 6.3.5. As discussed in Chapter 7, it would be beneficial for overall model
performance to optimize the hyperparameters for each area individually.

A.5
Feature Analysis
Additional information, graphs and tables to support the feature analysis in Section 6.4 in the
report.
A.5.1
Feature Permutation
To perform the feature permutation analysis, each single feature is individually shuffled and the
model predictions are performed with the feature shuffled. The idea is, that given the temporal
dependence in the data, the model performance should drop if a feature that is important for
the predictions is randomly shuffled. Features that cause the largest drop in performance can
thus be considered the most important features for the model. The feature permutation analysis
applied in this project did not lead to satisfying results, as no feature stands out to be significant
in the analysis and all features closely follow the baseline values. The reason for this can
be allocated to high correlation between features as well as the inherited uncertainty of how
different a shuffled feature is from the initial feature. Figure A.34 shows the results of the model
performances for each day in all three areas with shuffled features. It can be seen, that there
is no difference between any of the shuffled prediction and the baseline prediction.
(a) Gulf of Riga
(b) Southern Kattegat
(c) Gotland Basin
Figure A.34: Feature permutation anaysis results on a daily level
130
Quantifying Plankton Populations in the Baltic Sea
==PAGE:149==
A.5.2
Backward Feature Analysis
To perform the backward feature analysis, each single feature is individually omitted from the
model training. Features that cause the largest drop in performance are then considered the
most important features for the model.
Figure A.35 shows the results of the model performances for each day in all three areas. It
can be seen, that the uncertainty of which features are most important increases the longer in
advance the prediction is made, due the reduced difference in feature importance with increas­
ing days. Furthermore, the importance slightly differs on a day­to­day basis, which could be
explained by random variations in the small testing dataset.
(a) Gulf of Riga
(b) Southern Kattegat
(c) Gotland Basin
Figure A.35: Backward feature analysis results on a daily level
